Abstract
Background The metabolic disorder, diabetes mellitus, is approaching epidemic proportions
globally with more than 422 million people suﬀering from the disease and continuous increas-
ing incidence and prevalence. Morbidity and mortality of diabetic patients are substantially
aggravated by macrovascular complications including cardiovascular disease and stroke as well
as microvascular complications including retinopathy, neuropathy, and nephropathy. Diabetic
nephropathy is one of the most devastating complications of diabetes and it is the single most
common cause of chronic kidney disease which can lead to transplantation or dialysis-dependent
end-stage kidney disease.
Aim and methods The aim of this thesis was to investigate the impact of diﬀerent diets/treat-
ments on gene expression in the kidneys of Göttingen Minipigs. Four diﬀerent study groups were
investigated; a group fed standard diet, a diet normalisation group fed a high fat diet eventually
changed to standard diet, a group fed a high fat diet and ﬁnally a group induced experimentally
with diabetes mellitus with streptozotocin and fed a high fat diet. The method for the investiga-
tion of the study was as follows; (i) RNA was puriﬁed from kidney tissue, (ii) RNA quality was
assessed, (iii) cDNA was synthesised, (iv) qPCR experiments were performed and (v) data was
processed and analysed. Additional physical and metabolic measurements provided by Berit
Østergaard Christoﬀersen, Peter Helding Kvist, and Rikke Lindgaard Thomsen were analysed
and included in this study.
Results The physical measurements of the minipigs establish that after 13 months of high fat
diet and streptozotocin induction the minipigs became obese with increased kidney weight and
increased glomerular area compared to the other diet groups. The metabolic measurements
provide evidence of increased fasting glucose and fructosamine in addition to increased inulin
clearance and decreased plasma creatinine levels in the diabetic group. No diﬀerence in albumin
and ﬁbrosis levels were detected between the groups. The gene expression measurements were
done on extracted RNA of very high quality. The expression proﬁle provided 10 signiﬁcant
deregulated genes (p<0.0005, fold change ≳2 and ≲-1.5). The genes are known to be involved
in cell proliferation, recruitment and activation of macrophages, vasodilation and constriction,
glucose transport and podocyte structure in the kidneys.
Conclusion The Göttingen minipig eﬀectively mimicked the early stages of human diabetic
nephropathy in physical and metabolic measurements. The signiﬁcant deregulated genes have
to some extend been associated with kidney function in diabetic human and animal studies.
There is great potential to improve and expand the study setup in order to get a more detailed
picture of the disease and to investigate therapeutic methods for novel treatments of DN.
Keywords: Diabetes mellitus, diabetic nephropathy, gene expression, Göttingen minipig,
genetics, kidney disease

Resumé
Baggrund Den metaboliske sygdom, diabetes mellitus, nærmer sig epidemiske proportioner
globalt med mere end 422 millioner mennesker ramt af sygdommen og en fortsat stigende
forekomst. Sygeligheden og dødeligheden blandt patienter med diabetes er væsentligt forværret
af makrovaskulære komplikationer såsom hjertekarsygdomme og slagtilfælde samt mikrovaskulære
komplikationer såsom retinopati, neuropati og nefropati. Diabetisk nefropati er en af de værste
komplikationer forårsaget af diabetes og den hyppigste årsag til kronisk nyresygdom, som kan
fører til nyresvigt med behov for dialyse eller transplantation.
Formål of metode Formålet med dette speciale var at undersøge hvilken indﬂydelse forskel-
ligt foder og forskellig behandling har på genekspressionen i nyrerne fra Göttingen minigrise. Fire
forskellige grupper indgik i studiet; en gruppe fodret med standard foder, en foder-normaliserings-
gruppe fodret med fedtholdigt foder i den første halvdel af studiet og standard foder i den
resterende halvdel, en gruppe fodret med fedtholdigt foder gennem hele studiet og endelig en
gruppe fodret med fedtholdigt foder samt kemisk induceret med diabetes ved hjælp af strepto-
zotocin. Metoden brugt til at undersøge indﬂydelsen var som følger: (i) RNA blev oprenset fra
nyrevæv, (ii) RNA-kvaliteten blev undersøgt, (iii) cDNA blev syntetiseret, (iv) qPCR eksperi-
menter blev udført og (v) data blev bearbejdet og analyseret. Supplerende fysiske of metaboliske
målinger blev stillet til rådighed af Berit Østergaard Christoﬀersen, Peter Helding Kvist og Rikke
Lindgaard Dette data blev også analyseret og medtaget i studiet.
Resultater De fysiske målinger af minigrisene fastsatte at efter 13 måneder med fedtholdigt
foder og streptozotocin induktion blev grisene overvægtige med øget nyrevægt og forstørrede
glomeruli sammenlignet med de andre grupper. De metaboliske målinger påviser en forøgelse af
glukose og fruktosamin i blodet samt en forøgelse af inulin-udskillelse og en nedgang i plasma
kreatinin i den diabetiske gruppe sammenlignet med de andre grupper. Der var ingen synlig
forskel på albumin- og ﬁbroseniveauerne blandt grupperne.
Genekspression-målingerne blev
foretaget med oprenset RNA af høj kvalitet.
Ekspressionsproﬁlen gav 10 signiﬁkant dereg-
ulerede gener (p<0.0005, fold ændring ≳2 og ≲-1.5). Generne er kendt for at være involveret i
celleproliferation, rekrutering og aktivering af makrofager, vasodilation og konstriktion, glukose
transport og podocyte struktur i nyrerne.
Konklusion Göttingen minigrisene efterlignede det tidlige stadie i menneskelig DN i de fysiske
og metaboliske målinger eﬀektivt. De signiﬁkant deregulerede gener er til en vis grad blevet
associeret med nyrefunktionen i menneske- og dyrestudier.
Der er et stort potentiale for at
forbedre og udvide studiet for at opnå et mere detaljeret billede af sygdommen og for at under-
søge terapeutiske metoder til nye behandlinger af DN.
Nøgleord: Diabetes mellitus, diabetisk nefropati, genekspression, Göttingen minigris,
genetik, nyresygdom

Preface and acknowledgements
This thesis represents the master project carried out from June to November 2018 at the De-
partment of Veterinary and Animal Sciences at the University of Copenhagen, Frederiksberg,
Denmark, under the supervision of associate professor Susanna Cirera and senior researcher
Kerstin Skovgaard. The thesis is the ﬁnal written product in my pursuit of a master’s degree in
biotechnology at the Technical University of Denmark.
I have a lot of people to thank for making this project a very exciting, interesting and pleasant
experience. First and foremost, I owe a great thank you to my supervisor Susanna Cirera who
has been the best and most helpful supervisor I could have asked for. Her enthusiasm and great
interest in the project was very encouraging and motivating. I would also like to thank my second
supervisor Kerstin Skovgaard for her help with the written part of the project. All corrections
and suggestions were greatly appreciated. A big thank you goes to the lab technicians, Charlotte
Bjørner Larsen, Tina Bahrt Neergaard Mahler and Minna Baron Jakobsen for their patient
guidance in the lab. Furthermore, I am very grateful for the expert advice, knowledge and
data provided by Peter Helding Kvist, Rikke Kaae Kirk, Rikke Lindgaard Thomsen, Merete
Fredholm, Berit Østergaard Christoﬀersen and Henrik Duelund Pedersen. My ﬁnal tribute goes
to the minipigs who involuntarily participated in this study; it could not have been done without
you.
Camilla Aarup Kristensen, November 2018

1
Aim and hypotheses
This master thesis investigates a Göttingen minipig model for lifestyle-related obesity and its
associated kidney complications. The overall aim is to investigate the impact of diﬀerent di-
ets/treatments on gene expression in the kidney to elucidate if the treatments aﬀect kidney
function.
The following hypotheses were tested:
1. A high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet will induce changes in the kidney transcriptome.
2. Experimental induction of diabetes mellitus (DM) with streptozotocin in minipigs fed a
high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet will induce speciﬁc changes in the kidney transcrip-
tome related to DM and DN in humans.
3. The glomerular ﬁltration rate of diabetes induced minipigs fed a high fat, fructose, and
cholesterol diet will be increased as seen in early stages of diabetic nephropathy in humans.
1
==PAGE:24==
==PAGE:25==
2
Background
This chapter will introduce the background of this study based on a literature review. The
chapter will mainly be focused on the disease, diabetes mellitus, and the sequela, diabetic
nephropathy. The chapter will contain information about why diabetes mellitus is an important
and interesting ﬁeld of research and the reason for choosing to investigate the disease in a porcine
model.
2.1
Diabetes mellitus
Diabetes mellitus (DM) is a chronic, metabolic disorder that aﬀects the ability of the pancreas to
produce, secrete and/or use the hormone insulin. It is a multifactorial disease, meaning that it is
caused by a combination of genetic and environmental factors. DM is characterised by elevated
levels of glucose in blood (hyperglycemia) and in urine (glycosuria).
Moreover, DM causes
disturbances in the metabolism of carbohydrates, lipids, and proteins. Patients suﬀering from
DM experience increased morbidity and mortality due to hyperglycemia. Generally, the injurious
eﬀects of hyperglycemia are separated into macrovascular complications (coronary artery disease,
peripheral arterial disease, and stroke) and microvascular complications (diabetic nephropathy,
neuropathy, and retinopathy) (Fain, 2009; Frandes et al., 2017; Woodcock and Bradley, 2007;
Fowler, 2011).
Several forms of DM exist. DM type 1 (T1DM) (commonly known as juvenile-onset diabetes)
is usually developed in childhood or early adulthood and is characterised by the pancreas’
inability to produce insulin. Injection of insulin is necessary and is used as the current treatment.
The loss of insulin production function is due to an autoimmune destruction of the pancreatic
β-cells by eﬀector lymphocytes (Chatzigeorgiou et al., 2009). DM type 2 (T2DM) (commonly
known as adult-onset diabetes) typically develop in late adulthood although cases observed in
children and young adults are increasing (Panagiotopoulos et al., 2018). T2DM is characterised
by a declined production of insulin and/or a declined response to insulin. T2DM is often related
to high body mass index and the initial management or treatment involves changes in diet and
exercise, and in some cases medication to increase production or uptake of insulin (Woodcock
and Bradley, 2007). T1DM and T2DM are the most common diabetes types accounting for 10%
and 80% of the cases, respectively. The remaining 10% represent more than 30 diﬀerent forms
of diabetes (Diabetesforeningen, 2018).
3
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2 | Background
Today, DM is a common chronic disease worldwide that has reached epidemic proportions
with an estimate of 422 million diseased adults worldwide in 2014. DM is the seventh leading
cause of death and is one of the biggest disease-challenges of this century (WHO, 2016; Zhang
et al., 2010; WHO, 2018). The number of people suﬀering from DM will continue to increase
globally due to growth of population size, ageing population and increasing prevalence of obesity
and sedentary lifestyle. Furthermore, people with diabetes require a lot of medication, have a
high probability of being hospitalised and are likely to require long-term medication (Zhang
et al., 2010). Zhang et al. (2010) estimates the annual global health expenditure for diabetes
in 2030 to be between 490 billion and 893 billion USD. The statistics reﬂect an increased need
for screening and early treatment of the disease in order to avoid its comorbidities such as
cardiovascular complications and renal diseases (Ossman, 2006).
2.2
Diabetic nephropathy
Renal diseases, such as diabetic nephropathy (DN), are very threatening consequences of DM.
DN is the chronic loss of kidney function and the physiologic key factors of the disease are
inﬂammation and ﬁbrosis (Suryavanshi and Kulkarni, 2017). The kidneys are complex organs
with several vital functions in the human body. The renal functions include excretion of waste
products, conservation of essential solutes, production of erythropoietin, regulation of the ﬂuid,
electrolyte and acid-base balance of the body to create a stable environment for tissue and
cell metabolism (Wallace, 1998; Kim and Kim, 2018). The functional elements of the kidneys
consist of an outer and inner cortex and an inner renal medulla, which contain the nephrons.
Approximately 1 million nephrons located in the cortex and medulla of the kidney are the
functional work units of the kidney (See Figure 2.1) (Wallace, 1998).
Figure 2.1 Normal kidney. The normal kidney has a medulla and cortex that contain the functional
ﬁltration unit of the kidney called the nephron. Figure from Britannica (2018).
Approximately 1L of blood is perfused through the kidneys per minute, which corresponds to
20% of the body’s cardiac output. The blood is ﬁltered through the glomerulus entering via the
aﬀerent arteriole and exiting through the eﬀerent arteriole. The eﬀerent arteriole is smaller than
the aﬀerent, causing the pressure to increase within the glomerulus, enhancing ﬁltration. The
capillaries of the glomerulus are lined by a glomerular basement membrane (GBM) sandwiched
between endothelial cells (endothelium) and podocytes (epithelium) (See Figure 2.2).
4
==PAGE:27==
2.2. Diabetic nephropathy
Figure 2.2 Glomerular capillary membrane and its major components: capillary endothelium,
basement membrane, and podocytes (epithelium). Arrows indicating the direction of ﬁltration. Figure
adapted from Luhidan et al. (2016).
The pores between epithelial cells (fenestrae) allow blood to be ﬁltered but obstruct cells from
entering. Furthermore, the GBM is negatively charged to repel negatively charged molecules,
such as albumin. The ﬁnal layer consists of a cell type called podocyte. The podocytes have
foot processes that wrap around the basement membrane leaving gaps called ﬁltration slits.
Once blood is ﬁltered through it is called ﬁltrate (Renin and Robinson, 1974). The ﬁltrate is
collected in the space between the glomerulus and the capsule surrounding the glomerulus called
Bowman’s space and leaves through the collecting duct (Fasano and Sequeira, 2017; Wallace,
1998).
The microvascular complication, DN, is the most common cause of kidney damage in DM
patients (Thomas and Viberti, 2006). DN is fundamentally triggered by glucose metabolism
defects (Patschan and Müller, 2016; Kanasaki et al., 2013). Once DN has developed it cannot
be reversed eﬃciently, and it is largely responsible for chronic kidney disease (CKD), dialysis-
dependent end stage renal disease and kidney failure (Vitova et al., 2017). Kidney failure is
classiﬁed in stages according to glomerular ﬁltration rate (GFR), which is a measure of the
amount of blood ﬁltered through the glomerulus per minute. The GFR of an individual can
be calculated from the results of a blood creatinine test, age, size and gender (Ossman, 2006).
The GFR of a healthy individual is typically ≥90 mL/min depending on age and gender. A
GFR < 90 mL/min is interpreted as decreased renal function and if GFR < 15 mL/min it is
indicative of severe kidney failure. In research, GFR is often estimated by inulin clearance, since
inulin is uniquely treated by the nephrons. Inulin is a polysaccharide belonging to a class of
dietary ﬁbers known as fructans. It is completely ﬁltered at the glomerulus but neither secreted
nor reabsorbed by the tubules, which allows the clearance of inulin to be used in research as
a highly accurate measure of GFR (Hernández Ocampo et al., 2010; Von Hendy-Willson and
Pressler, 2011). However, unlike creatinine, inulin is not naturally present in the body. This is
an advantage, since the intravenously injected amount will be known. Inulin is excreted renally
and is not metabolised in the circulation.
Thus, the secreted amount in the urine provides
information on kidney function. Despite the advantage, the method is more complicated than
measuring the naturally occurring creatinine due to the necessity of injection (Mensink et al.,
2015). Once the GFR and thus the renal function starts to decline, it can result in increased
frequency of renal and extra-renal events, such as cardiovascular disease (CVD). Patients with
5
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2 | Background
diabetes are more than twice as likely to die from vascular causes as those without diabetes
(Pálsson and Patel, 2014). For this reason, slowing the decline of renal function is one of the
main areas of focus in diabetic nephropathy research (Kanasaki et al., 2013). As mentioned
earlier, DM causes hyperglycemia and glycosuria, which can cause non-enzymatic glycation of
proteins in the blood allowing proteins to cross the GBM and enter the urine along with the
glucose molecules resulting in proteinuria. The excess of plasma protein in the urine, such as
albumin, contributes to the replacement of smooth muscle cells of the arteriolar wall, making the
arterioles hardening and lose their elasticity. The hardening of the aﬀerent and eﬀerent arterioles
is called hyalinosis and is characteristic for DN (Cortes and Mogensen, 2006). Especially the
thickening of the eﬀerent arteriole is speciﬁc for DN and cause an increase in blood pressure
(hypertension) in the glomerulus and increased GFR (hyperﬁltration). The ﬁve stages of kidney
failure and their signature pathophysiology is outlined in Figure 2.3 and further described in
the following section.
The ﬁrst step of DN in general lasts up to approximately ﬁve years after onset of DM and is
characterised by normal or increased GFR, increased kidney size (hypertrophy) by approximately
20% and an increased renal plasma ﬂow. Albuminuria and blood pressure generally remain in
the normal range at this stage (Vujicic et al., 2012). Second stage is sometimes referred to as the
"quiet stage" because there are no clinical signs of the disease in this stage. In response to the
increased GFR the mesangial cells in the glomerulus start to secrete more extracellular matrix
(ECM) proteins expanding the glomerulus and thickening the GBM. GBM thickening result in
expansion of the fenestrae and the ﬁltration slits making the membrane more permeable, which
causes more plasma proteins to leak through. GFR return to normal values. Stage three is
also known as the microalbuminuria stage, which is the ﬁrst clinically detectable sign of DN.
Blood pressure may also increase in this stage. Stage four is the irreversible stage. As the
glomerulus becomes more and more damaged and the ﬁltration system becomes impaired, the
functionality of the kidneys is reduced and the GFR start to decrease rapidly, and proteinuria
and blood pressure increase above normal values (Cortes and Mogensen, 2006; Murphy et al.,
1999; Susztak et al., 2003; Hasslacher, 2002; Vujicic et al., 2012). In stage ﬁve, kidney function
is nearly lost, and dialysis or kidney transplant is necessary for survival (Hasslacher, 2002).

Figure 2.3 Stages of renal failure. The initial pathophysiology of DN is an increased GFR and
expansion of the glomerulus.
Second stage is characterised by mesangial expansions and thickening
of the glomerular basement membrane (GBM) and microalbuminuria. In stage three, the GFR start
declining.
In step four, proteinuria develops, and blood pressure increase.
In the ﬁnal step, kidney
function is lost, and transplantation or dialysis is required. Abbreviations: GFR, glomerular ﬁltration
rate; GBM, glomerular basement membrane.
In a study by Feng et al. (2015), the pro-inﬂammatory factors; IL6, CXCL2, MCP1, CCL5,
and VCAM1 as well as several toll-like receptors (TLR2, TLR4, TLR5, TLR7, TLR8, and
TLR11) and the macrophage marker CD68 were found to be signiﬁcantly up-regulated in DM
renal tissue of minipigs versus control pigs. Especially toll-like receptors have an important role
in the activation of NF-κβ signalling, which ultimately lead to metabolic inﬂammation in kidney
tissue and development of renal damage (Feng et al., 2015). Furthermore, oxidative stress has
been described as a major mechanism involved in the progression of DN. Oxidative stress is
caused by chronic hyperglycemia by increasing the production of ROS (Amaral et al., 2018).
6
==PAGE:29==
2.2. Diabetic nephropathy
Studies by Agarwal et al. (2012) showed that the formation of ROS could be reduced in rats
by regular exercise. The reduction in ROS is associated with reduction in TNF-α and NF-κβ
expression.
Evidence from many studies establish the importance of TGF-β/SMAD signalling. The sig-
nalling pathway plays a role in regulating epithelial-mesenchymal transition (EMT). EMT is a
process by which epithelial cells undergo transition to become mesenchymal cells. The transition
lead to extracellular matrix production and contribute to the progression of renal ﬁbrosis (Liu,
2010; Yanagita, 2012; Cho, 2010).
TGF-β has also been associated with cell cycle regulation in DN, by stimulating the expression
of the cyclin-dependent kinase (CDK) inhibitors, CDKN1A and CDKN1B. Both CDK-inhibitors
mediate G1-phase arrest by binding to and inhibiting cyclin/CDK complexes. Mesangial cells
arrested in the G1-phase induce hypertrophy (Wolf, 2000). The role of CDKN1A was studied by
Kuan et al. (1998) who showed the association between increased levels of CDKN1A in glucose-
induced mesangial cell hypertrophy in vivo and in vitro. Moreover, it was shown that despite
increased expression of TGF-β, diabetic mice with genetically deleted CDKN1A, did not develop
glomerular hypertrophy (Al-Douahji et al., 1999).
7
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 2 | Background
2.2.1
Inﬂammatory pathways of diabetic nephropathy
Diabetic nephropathy is a complex disease due to the various cell types linked to the functional
and structural impairment of the kidneys, and many aspects of the mechanism remain unclear
(Wada and Makino, 2013). In recent years, the critical role of inﬂammation in the pathogenesis
of DN has become evident.
Studies have demonstrated that cytokines, chemokines, growth
factors, adhesion molecules, nuclear factors as well as immune cells as monocytes, lymphocytes
and macrophages play an important part in DM complications, such as DN (Duran-Salgado,
2014; Feng et al., 2015). Due to the multiple pathways leading to renal injury and the many
cell types and molecules involved, it is unlikely that one single molecule is suﬃcient for the
development of DN. However, hyperglycemia is the major driving force in the progression of DN
and three major pathways in the progression have been established (See Figure 2.4).

Figure 2.4 Inﬂammatory pathways in the pathogenesis of diabetic nephropathy. Hyper-
glycemia promotes DN through various inﬂammatory pathways including intracellular signalling, gly-
cation, oxidative stress and hyperﬁltration. NF-κβ plays a key role in the transcriptional induction of
inﬂammatory cytokines, chemokines and adhesion molecules, which promote renal inﬁltration of inﬂam-
matory cells. Figure adapted from Wada and Makino (2013).
8
==PAGE:31==
2.2. Diabetic nephropathy
The ﬁrst pathway is the abnormality of intracellular metabolism, such as the activation of the
polyol and protein kinase C (PKC) pathways. A hyperglycemic environment causes mesangial
cells to accumulate polyols and produce excessive matrix proteins.
It has been shown that
inhibition of aldose reductase, which is a key regulating enzyme of the polyol pathway, prevent
the development and progression of proteinuria and thickening of the GBM is diabetic animals
(Hashimoto et al., 2011). The PKC pathways has been associated with increased permeability of
the GBM, extracellular matrix synthesis, cell growth and apoptosis, angiogenesis, and cytokine
activation and inhibition (Geraldes and King, 2010).
The second major pathway is the formation of reactive oxygen species (ROS) and advanced
glycation end-products (AGEs). The large excess of glucose molecules in the lumen of the blood
vessels of diabetic patients cause endothelial cells of the vessels to take up glucose molecules,
since there is no need for insulin for this to happen (Artwohl et al., 2007). When epithelial
cells take up high amounts of glucose, the cells produce a lot of energy and as a bi-product;
ROS. The increase in ROS lead to formation of AGEs and stimulation of protein kinase C
(PKC) activity.
This leads to an increased production of growth factors, such as vascular
endothelial growth factor (VEGF), resulting in angiogenesis and cell growth (Goto et al., 1993).
Furthermore, inﬂammatory transcription factors, including nuclear factor kappa-light-chain-
enhancer of activated B cells (NF-κβ), are activated in the hyperglycaemic environment (Wada
and Makino, 2013).
Activation of NF-κβ mediates transcriptional induction of various pro-
inﬂammatory cytokines, such as tumor necrosis factor alpha (TNF-α), interleukin-1 (IL-1), and
interleukin-6 (IL-6) (Zhang and Sun, 2015) as well as chemokines and adhesion molecules, such
as CC chemokine ligand 2 (CCL2) and intercellular adhesion molecule 1 (ICAM1) (Wada and
Makino, 2013).
Monocytes are attracted by monocyte-speciﬁc chemokines and transmigrate
into the tissue following an endothelial-bound gradient of chemokine molecules and become
macrophages (Mora and Navarro, 2006; Gu et al., 1999). The accumulation of macrophages as
well as monocytes, T-cells, and ﬁbroblast cause inﬂammation and ﬁbrosis, which are key factors
in the pathophysiology of DN (Suryavanshi and Kulkarni, 2017; Tesch, 2010; Wada and Makino,
2013).
The third major pathway is glomerular hyperﬁltration and hypertension. Hyperﬁltration is
caused by changes in the aﬀerent and eﬀerent arterioles. Studies suggest that changes in the
aﬀerent arterioles are caused by increased sodium re-absorption mediated by sodium-glucose
cotransporter-2 (SGLT2). The decline in circulating volumes of sodium is sensed in the glomeru-
lus and as a response adenosine generation is reduced to cause aﬀerent arteriolar vasodilatation
and increased GFR (Sasson and Cherney, 2012). Glomerular hyperﬁltration is furthermore as-
sociated with the activation of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system (RAAS) (Hainsworth
et al., 2002).
The RAAS is a hormone system that regulates blood pressure and ﬂuid balance. It plays
an important role in the initiation and progression of diabetic nephropathy. The system is set
in motion as compensation for low blood pressure or low serum sodium.
Initially, the liver
produces angiotensinogen, which is converted to angiotensinogen I (AngI) through the action of
the enzyme, renin. Renin is produced in the kidneys as a response to low ﬂuid volume moving
through the nephrons. Once AngI, present in the blood, ﬂows through the lungs and comes in
contact with ACE, it is converted to AngII. AngII acts on the adrenal gland and causes it to
release the steroid hormone, aldosterone. Aldosterone aﬀect the collecting duct of the nephrons,
causing them to retain water, and thus salt, resulting in increased blood pressure (See Figure
2.5) (De et al., 2006; Currie et al., 2010).
9
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 2 | Background

Figure 2.5 The renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system. Regulation of blood pressure and ﬂuid
balance is set in motion as a response to low blood volume ﬂowing through the nephrons. Abbreviation:
ACE, angiotensinogen-converting enzyme. Figure adapted from Rad (2006).
Research has shown that blockade of the RAAS is successful in retarding progressive loss of
renal function. Blockade of the RAAS can be done by either angiotensin-converting enzyme
(ACE) inhibitors or angiotensin II receptor blockers (De et al., 2006; Suryavanshi and Kulkarni,
2017; Currie et al., 2010).
2.2.2
Renal ﬁbrosis
Kidney ﬁbrosis is a common outcome of progressive kidney disease. It results in signiﬁcant de-
struction of normal kidney structure accompanied by functional deterioration. Several pathogenic
pathways lead to inﬂammation. Inﬂammation can either cause kidney repair when regulated or
ﬁbrosis if uncontrolled. Fibrosis is caused by the wound healing response to injury, activated in
order to maintain the original tissue composition and function. Inﬂammation is required in the
process but can essentially have two outcomes. Either the inﬂammatory response results in a
normal tissue repair process where structure and function is regained or the tissue repair pro-
cess becomes abnormal and uncontrolled leading to progressive ﬁbrosis and loss of structure and
function. Therapeutic interventions to balance the inﬂammatory response in order to inhibit
progressive kidney ﬁbrosis is thus of great potential (Kanasaki et al., 2013).
The proﬁbrotic cytokine, transforming growth factor-beta (TGFB), plays an important role
in the progression of renal ﬁbrosis, and it has been shown that latent TGFB has anti-proﬁbrotic
eﬀects (See Figure 2.6) (Kanasaki et al., 2013; Yanagita, 2012; Cho, 2010).
10

Figure 2.6 TGFB and BMP7 signalling. TGFB-dependent signalling promotes ﬁbrosis and inhibits
inﬂammation, whereas BMP7-mediated signalling inhibits ﬁbrosis, inﬂammation and cell death. Abbre-
viations: BMP7, bone morphogenetic protein 7; BMPRI/II, bone morphogenetic protein receptor I/II;
TGFB, transforming growth factor-beta; TBRI/II, TGFB receptor I/II; P, phosphate. Figure adapted
from Yanagita (2012).
The progression of ﬁbrosis happens through a signalling pathway, known as the TGFB/SMAD
signalling pathway. The system is functioning by a downstream cascade of Smads (The Smads
received their name as a contraction of the names of the C. elegans Sma and Drosophila Mad
(Derynck et al., 1998) and stands for "mothers against decapentaplegic"). The BMP system
is activated when TGFB binds to its receptor, TGFB receptor II (TBRII), which activates
the TGFB receptor I (TBRI) kinase. Once activated, TBRI phosphorylates receptor-regulated
Smads (R-Smads), Smad2 and Smad3. Phosphorylated Smad2 and Smad3 form an oligomeric
complex with Smad4. The complex regulates target gene transcription, including smad7 once it
is translocated into the nucleus. Smad7 is an inhibitory Smad and regulates the activation of R-
Smads negatively. BMP7 is a member of the TGFB superfamily that antagonises the proﬁbrotic
function of TGFB. BMP7 bind to its receptors, BMPRI and BMPRII. Ligand binding causes
BMPRII to phosphorylate BMPRI, which is then able to phosphorylate the R-Smads, Smad1, 5
and 8. By forming a complex with Smad4, the complex inhibits ﬁbrosis, inﬂammation and cell
death (Yanagita, 2012).
Extensive research has been done to provide therapeutic solutions for renal diseases, and
BMP7 has played an important role (Wang et al., 2003). In 2003, Zeisberg et al. provided
the evidence that administration of BMP7 to patients with chronic kidney injury led to repair
of severely damaged renal tubular epithelial cells, in association with reversal of chronic renal
injury (Yanagita, 2012). Furthermore, Wang (2006) concluded that maintenance of renal BMP7
during the evolution of diabetic nephropathy reduces diabetic renal injury, especially podocyte
dropout. Podocytes are one of the key structural components of the ﬁltration barrier of the
glomerulus and dysfunction, injury or loss of podocytes aﬀect the permeability of the GBM
(Wang et al., 2017) A high number of studies have demonstrated correlation between podocyte
loss and proteinuria, indicating that podocyte loss is a potential key factor in the progression of
DN (Lin and Susztak, 2016). The podocyte foot processes interlock with neighbouring podocyte
11
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 2 | Background
foot processes to encircle the glomerular capillaries leaving evenly spaced areas covered by slit-
diaphragm proteins that allow for podocyte-to-podocyte contact. A well-known slit-diaphragm
protein, nephrin (NPHS1) function as a structural and functional signalling protein in podocytes
and is involved in podocyte survival. Dysregulation of NPHS1 may lead to ﬂattening, widening,
and retraction of the podocytes’ foot-processes thus weakening the ﬁltration barrier of the GBM,
resulting in albuminuria (Lin and Susztak, 2016).
2.2.3
Characteristic glomerular changes of diabetic nephropathy
Based on biopsy material from patients with clinical DN, glomerular lesions represent the most
striking and consistent change. DN is histologically characterised by four types of lesions: (i)
mesangial expansion (known as hypertrophy), (ii) thickened glomerular basement membranes,
(iii) hyalinosis of aﬀerent and eﬀerent arterioles and (iv) podocyte injury (See Figure 2.7).
Widening of the mesangium, due to increased accumulation of matrix is the earliest detectable
change (Najaﬁan et al., 2011; Riphagen et al., 2015).

==PAGE:35==
2.3. Animal models of diabetes mellitus
2.3
Animal models of diabetes mellitus
Experimental animal models are essential tools for investigating and understanding a multifac-
torial disease such as DM (Chatzigeorgiou et al., 2009). Two of the most popular rodent models
that spontaneously develop diseases with similarities to human T1DM are the non-obese diabetic
(NOD) mouse and the bio-breeding (BB) rat (Rees and Alcolado, 2005). A genetic mutation,
which aﬀect the selection of T-lymphocytes is the likely cause of spontaneous development of
DM (Gvazava et al., 2018). Furthermore, nonhuman primates as well as porcine models have
been investigated (Wang et al., 2014; Larsen and Rolin, 2004).
2.3.1
Non-obese diabetic mouse model
The NOD mouse model has greatly advanced our knowledge of the pathogenesis of T1DM.
The extensive research has demonstrated that CD4 or CD8 T cells are suﬃcient to cause β-
cell destruction. Important advantages of the NOD mouse model for DM include access to the
pancreas at pre-clinical stages, the ability to breed and genetically manipulate animals, and the
capacity to employ intervention strategies (Solomon and Sarvetnick, 2004).
2.3.2
Bio-breeding rat model
The BB rat spontaneously develops a heritable syndrome that closely resembles human T1DM
(Rossini et al., 1984). In common with the NOD mouse, the pancreatic islets are subjected to
an immune attack with T cells, B cells, macrophages and natural killer cells being recruited to
the insulitis and the susceptibility gene is located within the major histocompatibility complex
(Li and Sun, 2010).
Besides the spontaneous autoimmune models, such as the NOD mouse and BB rat, T1DM
have been studied in chemically induced-, genetically induced- and virally induced models.
T2DM is frequently studied in obese models, such as the Otsuka Long-Evans Tokushima Fatty
rat and the New Zealand obese mouse, or in a non-obese mouse model (King, 2012).
2.3.3
Rhesus monkeys
Another animal model reported to reﬂect the properties of human DM and DN is the rhesus
monkey. Rhesus monkeys treated with streptozotocin become diabetic and furthermore develop
diabetic complications such as DN (Wang et al., 2014). The rhesus monkey as well as other
nonhuman primates can serve as good models of DM because have a close phylogenetic rela-
tionship to humans and they develop many similar pathological abnormalities when diabetes
is developed spontaneous or induced compared to human diabetic patients. For this reason,
nonhuman primates are an eﬃcient preclinical model of DM (Qiao et al., 2009).
13
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 2 | Background
2.3.4
Porcine model
Despite the wide variation of well characterised rodent models available for studying diabetes,
large animals are relevant and beneﬁcial as complement to rodent models. The pig is especially
useful because many organs as well as physiological and pathophysiological responses resemble
those of the human. Especially the pharmacokinetic similarities between pig and human and the
Figure 2.8 The Göttingen
minipig.
Figure from Elle-
gaard (2018a).
similar size of the pancreas are of special relevance in the study
of DM (Larsen and Rolin, 2004). The Göttingen minipig is the
smallest purpose-bred minipig available for research, weighing
only ~35kg when fully mature (See Figure 2.8).
It has white
skin, sparse hair, stable phenotype and well-characterised genet-
ics making it a reliable model for pharmacology, pharmacodynam-
ics, pharmacokinetics, toxicology, experimental surgery and fun-
damental science. Therefore, it is extensively used for biomedical
research when non-rodent models are required (Ellegaard, 2018a).
The Göttingen minipig is the result of crossbreeding between the
Vietnamese Potbelly pig, the Minnesota minipig and the German
Landrace (See Figure 2.9) (Ellegaard, 2018b). The crossbreeding
resulted in the characteristic small size of the Göttingen minipig,
making it easy to handle even when fully grown, which makes it
particularly suitable for long term studies of metabolic and kid-
ney diseases, such as DM and DN (Larsen and Rolin, 2004; Feng
et al., 2015).

Another factor making the pig a good model for the study of DM is its nutritional requirements
as well as its gastrointestinal physiology, which are very similar to that of humans. Both species
depend highly on dietary quality due to the gut microbiota’s relatively minor role in modifying
ingested nutrients. Feeding patterns are managed more easily in pigs that in rodents, which
require a more continuous feeding pattern (Miller and Ullrey, 1987; Larsen and Rolin, 2004).
Limitation associated with the use of porcine models in long term studies are mostly due to
handling and economy. However, the many advances outweigh the expense. Overall, the pig
has great potential as animal model of DM to identify mechanisms that lead to the development
of diabetic complications and to develop and test new therapeutic approaches (Bellinger et al.,
2006).
The porcine pancreas has many similarities to that of the human and produce insulin that only
diﬀer by a single amino acid at the 30 position of the β-chain. However, spontaneous diabetes
rarely occur in pigs and the disease must be induced experimentally (Redwan et al., 2016; Larsen
and Rolin, 2004). Induction of diabetes can be done chemically with Streptozotocin (STZ), which
is a 1-methyl-1-nitrosourea linked to position C2 of D-glucose. The glucose molecule (in deoxy
form) of the structure allows the uptake of STZ into the β-cells by the GLUT2 receptor in the

==PAGE:37==
2.4. Animal models of diabetic nephropathy
plasma membrane and the methylnitrosourea moiety is thought to exert the cytotoxic eﬀect. The
compound is diabetogenic due to selective destruction of the insulin-producing β-cells resulting
from necrosis leading to a reduced β-cell mass and insulin insuﬃciency (See Figure 2.10). Insulin
insuﬃciency leads to chronic hyperglycemia, which is strongly correlated with many DM-related
microvascular complications, including retinopathy, nephropathy, and neuropathy (Elsner et al.,
2000; Wu and Yan, 2015; Campos, 2012).

2.4
Animal models of diabetic nephropathy
As described in the previous section, a number of animal models for diabetes mellitus are
available for research. Rodents are a popular choice in the study of DM, unfortunately, they
tend to be resistant to DN development (Betz and Conway, 2016). However, Hudkins et al. has
shown that the relatively uncommon black and tan, brachyuric (BTBR) mouse develop similar
pathological features of human DN including arteriolar hyalinosis, mesangial expansion, and
reduction in podocyte number. Draw backs of using the BTBR mouse is the level of diﬃculty in
breeding and its high mortality rate beyond 24 weeks of age, making it diﬃcult to model more
advanced stages of nephropathy (Betz and Conway, 2016; Hudkins et al., 2010).
Non-rodent models have also been used in the study of DN. Following induction of T1DM
by administration of alloxan, dogs develop glomerular lesions within 2 years (Betz and Conway,
2016). Pigs have been used to model early glomerular lesions of human DN and test therapeutics.
Studies have found pigs to show early changes related to DN manifested as GBM thickening, and
reduction in podocyte number, making the porcine model useful for evaluating the initial stages
of DN (Maile et al., 2014). In 2002, Hainsworth et al. concluded that the diabetes induced pig
model is useful for early intervention and mechanistic studies of diabetes-associated microvas-
cular changes, due to ﬁndings of signiﬁcant changes in retinal capillary basement membrane
thickening in diabetic pigs compared to control groups.
Large animals have no record of showing advanced features of human DN, such as ﬁbrosis or
decline in renal function (Betz and Conway, 2016).

==PAGE:38==

3
Theory of the methods
The purpose of this chapter is to introduce and describe the theory and principles behind the
methods used in the project. This will elucidate why the speciﬁc methods are suﬃcient to reject
or accept the hypothesis of this thesis, and why each step is of great importance.
3.1
RNA isolation
In this project, RNA was isolated from tissue by the RNeasy Mini Kit (Qiagen). The kit is
based on technology that combines selective binding properties of a silica-based membrane with
centrifugation.
The tissue samples are initially lysed and homogenised in the presence of a
denaturing guanidine-thiocyanatecontaining buﬀer, which immediately inactivates RNases to
ensure puriﬁcation of intact RNA. Appropriate binding conditions are ensured by addition of
ethanol.
The sample is applied to a spin column, where RNA binds to the membrane and
contaminants are washed away. Finally, RNA is eluted in RNase-free water. The procedure
allows puriﬁcation of RNA molecules longer than 200 nucleotides. The procedure provides an
enrichment for 18S and 28S mRNA since many RNAs < 200 nucleotides (such as 5.8S rRNA,
5S rRNA, and tRNAs, which together comprise 15-20% of total RNA) are selectively excluded.
(Qiagen, 2012b). During the RNA isolation, DNase treatment was performed. DNase treatment
is necessary for certain RNA applications, such as qPCR analysis, which are sensitive to very
small amounts of DNA (i.e. unspeciﬁc binding to any double-stranded DNA can occur) (Bustin
et al., 2009).
3.2
RNA assessment
Once RNA has been puriﬁed from the kidney tissue it is important to determine the quantity of
RNA and whether the quality is adequate for qPCR purposes. Traces of extraction components,
DNA and protein contamination, and RNA degradation can dramatically aﬀect the quality of
qPCR data (Agilent, 2012).
17
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 3 | Theory of the methods
3.2.1
RNA quantity
Quantiﬁcation of RNA in the extracted samples is important, because it is advisable that ap-
proximately the same amounts of RNA is used for the cDNA synthesis when comparing diﬀer-
ent samples (Bustin et al., 2009). In this project, the NanoDropTM 1000 Spectrophotometer
(Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc) was used, but other methods exist, such as microﬂuidic analysis (Ag-
ilent Technologies’ Bioanalyzer, Bio-Rad Laboratories’ Experion), capillary gel electrophoresis
(Qiagen’s QIAxcel), or ﬂuorescent dye detection (Ambion/Applied Biosystems’ RiboGreen).
Since each method will produce diﬀerent results, it is important to only compare samples using
one method (Bustin et al., 2009).
3.2.2
RNA quality
A) The purity of RNA can be measure by a spectrophotometer by determining the ratio of ab-
sorbance at 260nm to the absorbance at 280nm (A260/A280). Pure RNA has a A260/A280 ratio
of 1.8-2.0. If the RNA is contaminated by proteins, it will have a high absorbance at 280nm and
thus a A260/A280 ratio lower than 1.8 (Geuther, 2007). Furthermore, the presence of residual
phenol will alter the ratio, which enables the absorbance ratio to provide an indication of the
RNA purity (Bustin et al., 2009). A260/A230 values for pure RNA are often higher than the
A260/A280 values and are commonly in the range of 2.0-2.2 (Geuther, 2007).
B) The integrity of RNA can be determined by visualising the discrete intact ribosomal 28S and
18S bands on an agarose gel after electrophoresis. By visually inspecting the ribosomal bands on
the gel, the degree of RNA degradation can be subjectively determined. Both ribosomal bands
should be sharp with no smearing. Furthermore, the ratio of 28S to 18S RNA should be 2:1
(Agilent, 2012). The RNA integrity can be further assessed by results from a microﬂuidic-based
rRNA analysis, such as the Experion platform (Bio-Rad) and the Bioanalyzer (Agilent Tech-
nologies). Both systems are based on an automated and miniaturised electrophoresis system,
realised by Lab-on-chip technology. The advantage of these methods is that they both deter-
mine RNA quality by a numerical system, which represents the integrity of RNA. The Experion
platform oﬀers an algorithm for calculating the RNA Quality Index (RQI) while the Bioanalyzer
platform oﬀers the RIN algorithm (RNA Integrity Number). The RQI and RIN are based on a
numbering system from 1 to 10, 1 being the most degraded RNA and 10 being the most intact
RNA (Riedmaier et al., 2010).
3.3
cDNA synthesis
cDNA synthesis is the generation of complementary DNA (cDNA) from an RNA template by
reverse transcription.
RNA and a primer complementary to the RNA is used to direct the
reverse transcriptases (RTs) to synthesise the ﬁrst strand of cDNA, which can then be used as
the template in a PCR to create and amplify double-stranded cDNA. The process of cDNA
synthesis is initiated with the RNA and primers present and a temperature around 65-70°C
in order to destroy the secondary structure of the RNA. The temperature is then dropped to
4°C in order for the primer to anneal to the RNA and to ensure proper conditions for the
reverse transcriptase enzyme. In the next step, the reverse transcriptase enzyme is added along
with deoxynucleotides (dNTPs) and reverse transcription buﬀer. The transcription occurs at
a temperature around 25-50°C. The ﬁnal step is to inactivate the enzyme at 70°C (See Figure
3.1) (Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc, 2018a). In a cDNA synthesis reaction, several primer options
are available. The ﬁrst option is to use oligo deoxythymidine (dT) priming, which is a primer
designed to bind to the 3’ poly-A tail of the RNA. The limitation of this priming method is
18
==PAGE:41==
3.4. Quantitative real-time PCR
that it can result in a 3’ end bias in the sample, due to where the primer binds. The method is
also very sensitive to partially degraded RNA. An alternative method, which can also be used
in conjunction with oligo-dT priming, is random priming. Random primers are oligonucleotides
with random base sequences. They are usually six nucleotides long and often referred to as
random hexamers. The random hexamers will bind all over the transcripts and combining them
with oligo-dT primers may result in higher RT eﬃciency and improved yield of cDNA (Agilent,
2012; Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc, 2018b). The ﬁnal primer option is gene-speciﬁc priming. These
primers are designed based on known sequences of the target RNA and will only make cDNA
for the speciﬁc target (Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc, 2018b).
 
Figure 3.1 cDNA synthesis. In the ﬁrst step of cDNA synthesis, the primers anneal to the RNA.
Then, the reverse transcriptase, in the presence of dNTPs and RT buﬀer, begin the transcription. After
approximately one hour, the transcription is done, and the temperature is raised to deactivate the enzyme.
Figure from Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc (2017).
3.4
Quantitative real-time PCR
Fluorescence-based quantitative real-time PCR (qPCR) is a technique in which target DNA
is ampliﬁed and the accumulated product is quantiﬁed by continuous monitoring throughout
the experiment.
The amount of ampliﬁed product is linked to ﬂuorescence intensity using
a ﬂuorescent reporter molecule. The ﬂuorescence intensity is proportional to the increase in
amplicon concentration during each ampliﬁcation cycle. The collected data is visualised in a
plot of sigmoidal ampliﬁcation curves of ﬂuorescence vs. cycle number for all samples (See Figure
3.2). The background is set at a common starting point (baseline correction) and a threshold
level of ﬂuorescence is set above the background but within the linear phase of ampliﬁcation.
The quantiﬁcation cycle (Cq) is the number of cycles at which the sample curve crosses the
threshold ﬂuorescence levels. The Cq is directly correlated to the starting target concentration
of the sample (Agilent, 2012; Bustin et al., 2009).
When using a semi-quantitative method
(relative quantiﬁcation), a standard curve is needed in order to calculate the PCR eﬃciency.
The standard curve is generated by plotting the log of the initial template copy number against
the Cq generated for each dilution and form a linear regression line (Agilent, 2012). Eﬃciency
can be can be calculated by Equation 3.1.
Efficiency = 10(−1/slope)−1) · 100
(3.1)
19
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 3 | Theory of the methods
Figure 3.2 Ampliﬁcation plot. qPCR run with 96 identical reactions and 40 cycles. Cq and reaction
endpoints marked. Figure from Agilent (2012).
There are several methods for performing qPCR. Two widely used methods are the dye-based
and the probe-based method.
3.4.1
The dye-based method
When using the dye-based method, a ﬂuorescent DNA binding dye is used, such as SYBR® Green
I or EvaGreen®. When the dye is free in the solution relatively low ﬂuorescence is displayed, but
when bound to double-stranded DNA, the ﬂuorescence increases by over 1000-fold. Thus, the
more double-stranded DNA present, the higher ﬂuorescent signalling, i.e. ﬂuorescence increases
proportionally to DNA concentration (See Figure 3.3).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
SYBR Green in solution 
emits low fluorescence 
SYBR Green-DNA complex with 
fluorescence emission 
Figure 3.3 SYBR® Green I chemistry. When the dye is unbound very little ﬂuorescence is emitted.
Once bound to double-stranded DNA the dye emits ﬂuorescence. Figure from Sigma-Aldrich (2017).
EvaGreen® is based on the same principle as SYBR® Green I but with a slight diﬀerence in
structure (See Figure 3.4).
Figure 3.4 EvaGreen® chemistry. The dye is constructed of two monomeric DNA-binding dyes linked
by a ﬂexible spacer. In the absence of DNA, the dye is inactive. When DNA is available, the looped
conformation shifts to a random conformation that is capable of binding to DNA to emit ﬂuorescence.
Figure from Biotium (2018).
20
==PAGE:43==
3.4. Quantitative real-time PCR
In this study, SYBR® Green I was used in the MxPro platform for optimisation purposes
while EvaGreen® was used in the ﬁnal semi-quantitative analysis in the Fluidigm platform.
A very important limitation of the dye-based technique is the inherent non-speciﬁcity, meaning
that ﬂuorescence will increase due to binding of any double-stranded DNA (Agilent, 2012).
The reaction speciﬁcity is thus solely determined by the primers, which has to be designed to
avoid non-speciﬁc binding, such as primer dimer formation. Detection of non-speciﬁc binding
is achieved by performing a melting curve analysis on the PCR products. After the PCR is
ﬁnished, the PCR products are slowly melted while ﬂuorescence is monitored, resulting in a
dissociation curve (also known as the melting curve).
As the DNA melts, the ﬂuorescence
decreases. The temperature at which DNA melts depend on its length and sequence. A PCR
product of homogeneous length and sequence will result in a single thermal transition in the
melting curve analysis. If a primer dimer is present in the sample, multiple thermal transitions
will be detected.
The dissociation curve is a plot of raw ﬂuorescence data units, R, versus
temperature (See Figure 3.5a). However, this data may appear diﬃcult to interpret, which is
why the dissociation curve is often represented as the negative ﬁrst derivative, R’(T), of the
raw ﬂuorescence against the temperature (See Figure 3.5b) (Agilent, 2012). This representation
results in a very profound peak, representing the thermal transition. The melting curve analysis
requires an evaluation of the melting curve peaks of all the samples and an evaluation of the
no template control (NTC) sample. A small, wide peak at a lower temperature is an indication
of primer-dimer formation. In the NTC sample, it is acceptable to observe a small amount of
primer-dimer, however, a corresponding peak in the sample ampliﬁcation plots makes the Cq
unreliable. Furthermore, if the Cq of the negative controls are 10 cycles higher than the Cq of
the samples, it is safe to assume that the results are accurate (Agilent, 2012; Bustin et al., 2009).
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.5 Dissociation curves. (a) Raw ﬂuorescence signal change plotted as a function of increasing
temperature. (b) The negative ﬁrst derivative of raw ﬂuorescence plotted as a function of increasing
temperature.
Well G7 is highlighted to show a small amount of primer-dimer in the NTC sample.
Abbreviation: NTC, no template control. Figure from Agilent (2012).

The probe-based method

3.4.3
The BioMarkTM HD system
The BioMarkTM HD system (Fluidigm) can be used with a dual labelled probe technique,
e.g. TaqMan® probes or with a dye-based technique. The use of DNA binding dyes for gene
expression analysis is a low-cost alternative to the probe-based method (Fluidigm, 2018). When
using a dye-based method with this system, it is recommended to use the SsoFastTM EvaGreen®
dye, which has several advantages over SYBR® Green I (Fluidigm, 2018). Compared to SYBR®
Green I, it is less inhibitory toward PCR and less likely to cause nonspeciﬁc ampliﬁcation. For
this reason, it can be used in higher concentrations resulting in a more stable PCR signal.
EvaGreen® dye is furthermore optimal for both qPCR and melting curve analysis, yielding
robust and reproducible results (Bio-Rad, 2018).
By using the BioMarkTM HD platform it is possible to measure the speciﬁc activity of a large
number of samples simultaneously and thereby obtain a comprehensive picture of the gene ex-
pression. The BioMarkTM HD system is based on an integrated ﬂuidic circuit (IFC) component
technique. Several chips with this technique exist with some varieties but the essential compo-
nents, such as sample and assay wells are common to all. The two IFCs used in this project
are the 96.96 Dynamic Array IFC and the Flex Six Gene Expression IFC. The 96.96 Dynamic
22
==PAGE:45==
3.4. Quantitative real-time PCR
Array IFC can be used to perform qPCR on 96 diﬀerent cDNA samples and interrogate 96 genes
on each of these samples (See Figure 3.7a). (Disease Center for Gastrointestinal Biology and
Disease, 2018).
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.7 (a) Fluidigm 96.96 Dynamic Array IFC and (b) Flex Six Gene Expression IFC.
Abbreviation: ICF, Integrated ﬂuidics circuit. Figure from Fluidigm (2014).
Individual cDNA samples and individual assays are loaded separately into wells on opposite
sides of the chip. The chip is designed to allow samples and assays to be mixed in a coordinated
system, where the ICF controllers push samples and assays through channels and by closing
ﬂuidic valves mix in individual compartments to create up to 9,216 reactions (Disease Center
for Gastrointestinal Biology and Disease, 2018). Before running a 96.96 Dynamic Array IFC,
a Flex Six gene expression IFC can be used for optimisation. This type of array has 12-assay-
by-12-sample partitions that can be run separately or together (See Figure 3.7b). The Flex Six
IFC is an economic chip compared to the 96.96 Dynamic Array IFC, making it ideal for target
selection and optimisation before running the 96.96 IFC (Fluidigm, 2014).
3.4.4
Sample normalisation using reference genes
Reference genes are important to include in a qPCR analysis to control for sample-to-sample
variations that are not a result of biological eﬀects. The reference gene must have a constant
expression under the experimental conditions of the assay.
Experimental veriﬁcation of the
expression levels of the reference genes is necessary. Any variation in Cq values are assumed to
be due to other sources of variation, such as reverse transcription eﬃciency or RNA puriﬁcation
yield diﬀerences (Agilent, 2012).
3.4.5
Primer evaluation
Primer evaluation is an important step in a qPCR analysis, since primer-dimer formation pro-
duces lower PCR eﬃciency in probe-based assays and may generate false positive in dye-based
assays with SYBR® Green I (Bustin et al., 2009). The Mx3005P platform (Agilent Technologies)
can be used for this purpose. Positive controls (cDNA sample replicates), are useful for moni-
toring assay variation. In addition to positive controls, negative controls should be included in
23
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 3 | Theory of the methods
the primer evaluation. No reverse transcription (-RT) controls (consisting of a cDNA synthesis
excluding reverse trascriptase) should be made as well as NTCs (with no cDNA added). -RT
controls should not be ampliﬁed, since it should not contain any cDNA. -RT and NTCs can be
used to detect and distinguish between unintended ampliﬁcations (primer-dimer) and the in-
tended PCR product (Bustin et al., 2009). In spite of primer evaluation prior to qPCR analysis,
the results are not guaranteed to be 100% matching in the ﬁnal qPCR experiment, since there
are diﬀerences in the platforms and dye-methods.

Chapter 4 | Materials and methods
4.1
Study setup and animals
Castrated, male Göttingen minipigs (Ellegaard Göttingen Minipigs A/S, Dalmose, Denmark)
aged six to seven months were submitted to diﬀerent diets. The minipigs were distributed in
four feeding groups fed once daily for 13 months (See Figure 4.2).
The minipigs were part
of a larger study (total n=84) investigating diﬀerent aspects of obesity and diabetes-related
complications. Two groups were not included in this study. The four feeding groups included in
this study were: SD, standard diet; FFC, high fat, fructose, cholesterol diet; FFC/SD, high fat,
fructose, cholesterol diet changed to a standard diet and FFCDIA, high fat, fructose, cholesterol
diet given to STZ treated minipigs (See Figure 4.2). The animals had access to fresh drinking
water at all times and straw was used as bedding material. After 13 months, the minipigs were
terminated and the kidneys were removed and weighed, and tissue was sampled from the kidneys
(Schumacher-Petersen, 2018).

also changed after ﬁve months from a 2% fat/fructose/cholesterol diet to a 1% fat/fructose/c-

The diabetic group (FFCDIA) was type 1 diabetes induced with STZ (60 mg/kg body weight)
administered intravenously once daily for three consecutive days. The diabetic animals were
treated once daily with long acting insulin (insulin glargine, Lantus®, Sanoﬁ-Aventis Deutschland
GmbH, Frankfurt am Main, Germany) subcutaneously immediately after daily diet ration was
oﬀered in the morning (Schumacher-Petersen, 2018). Furthermore, ﬂuorescein isothiocyanate-
inulin (FITC-Inulin) was used as a plasma marker to evaluate glomerular ﬁltration rate and was
given as an intravenous bolus through the intravenous catheter (TdB Consultansy AB, Sweden.
Cat. No. FI.) For more details see Appendix A.
Three to eight weeks before termination, blood was sampled in plain serum tubes and EDTA
coated tubes. Animals were fasted overnight before blood sampling and termination. Total
body fat percentage (BF%) was estimated one to six weeks before termination using dual x-ray
absorptiometry (Lunar prodigy, GE Heathcare, Brøndby, Denmark). The circulating biomark-
ers; plasma glucose, fructosamine and creatinine were quantiﬁed using an autoanalyzer Cobas
6000® (Roche A/S, Hvidovre, Denmark.) Inulin clearance was estimated through a FITC-Inulin
assay (For more information see PhD thesis by Schumacher-Petersen (2018) and Appendix A).
Fibrosis and glomerular area was measured and quantiﬁed. Data was provided by Berit Øster-
gaard Christoﬀersen (personal communication, November, 2018), (Thomsen et al., 2018). The
above-mentioned process was completed prior to this master project.

Chapter 4 | Materials and methods
For the purpose of this study, 42 samples including the inner and outer cortex of the left
kidney were provided (One FFC sample was lost prior to this project) (See ﬁgure 4.3).
39
kidney samples were snap frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at -80°C and 3 samples were
conserved in RNAlater.
Figure 4.3 Kidney sampling from the inner and outer cortex of the left kidney. One half of
the sample was snap frozen and the other half was conserved in RNA later. The sampling location in the
kidney was the same as the location for histology sampling.
4.2
RNA extraction and DNase treatment
Kidney tissue samples (n=42) were homogenised using GentleMACSTM Octo Dissosiator (Mil-
tenyi Biotec) and RNA was extracted using the RNeasy® Mini Kit (Qiagen).
The protocol
suggested by Qiagen was followed with minor modiﬁcations in regard to volumes (See Appendix
B). 60mg kidney tissue samples were homogenised on the GentleMacs in M-tubes with 1.2mL
RLT buﬀer and 20µL β-Mercaptoethanol. Furthermore, 5µL anti-foam (Sigma) was added to
avoid foam. 600mL homogenate was transferred to an Eppendorf tube and centrifuged (3 min,
200rpm). The supernatant was transferred to a clean Eppendorf tube and one volume of 70%
EtOH was added before transferring to an RNeasy spin column. Several steps of washing and dry
centrifugation were conducted in accordance to the Quick-Start Protocol Part 1 (See Appendix
B). The On-Column DNase digestion manual, described in the Qiagen Quick-Start Protocol
Part 2, was followed during washing of the RNA samples in the RNeasy Mini Spin Columns
(See Appendix B) (Qiagen, 2012a).
4.3
RNA quality assessment and quantiﬁcation
Traces of extraction components, DNA and protein contamination, and RNA degradation can
drastically aﬀect the quality of qPCR. Therefore, the quality of the extracted RNA was thor-
oughly assessed. The quality (purity and integrity) of the RNA was assessed in three ways:
A) NanoDropTM 1000 Spectrophotometer (Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc). A260/A280 ratios of the
RNA were examined in order to exclude protein contaminations. A260/A280 ratios of 1.8-2.0
were accepted. A260/A230 ratios were assessed in order to exclude chemical contaminations.
28
==PAGE:51==
4.4. cDNA synthesis
A260/A230 ratios of 2.0-2.2 were accepted. The Nanodrop spectrophotometer was also used to
determine the quantity of RNA. Samples of lower quantity than 500 ng/µL were up-concentrated
in a Speedvac system to achieve the desired concentration for cDNA synthesis.
B) A gel electrophoresis was run to check for degraded RNA. RNA samples of 500ng and
1000ng were run on a 1.4% agarose minigel at 100V for 20 min.
C) ExperionTM Automated Electrophoresis System (Bio-Rad). 100ng/µL DNase treated RNA
(1µL) was loaded to the Experion chip and the quick guide suggested by Bio-rad was followed (See
Appendix C). The RNA quality indicator (RQI) was assigned to each of the RNA extractions.
Acceptable quality was above seven (Bio-Rad, 2010a).
4.4
cDNA synthesis
Complementary DNA (cDNA) was synthesised from RNA samples. 1 µL of RNA (500 ng/µL)
was mixed with 0.5µL of oligo-dT:Random Primer 1:3 (0.5 µgr/µL) (TAG Copenhagen) and
1µL sterile distilled, RNase free water.
Afterwards, the mix was heated for 5 min at 70°C
and immediately chilled on ice for 5 min. To each sample was added 3.5µL sterile distilled,
RNase free water, 2µL 5x Improm II buﬀer (Promega), 0.5µL dNTP mix (10mM) (Amersham-
Pharmacia), 0.25µL RNasin Ribonuclease inhibitor 40u/µL (Promega), 2.5µL MgCl2 (25mM)
and 0.5µL ImProm II (Promega). The samples were incubated at room temperature (rt) for 5
min and then heated at 42°C for one hour. Finally, the enzyme was inactivated at 70°C for 15
min cDNA was stored at -80°C. The cDNA was diluted 1:16 for use in qPCR experiments. For
details, see protocol in Appendix D.
4.5
Quantitative real-time PCR
4.5.1
Selection of genes
For the qPCR experiment, 92 genes of interest and 4 reference genes were chosen. A list of
76 genes related to DN was provided by Rikke Lindgaard Thomsen (See Appendix E). The
remaining 16 genes were selected based on relevant literature (See Table 4.2).

Chapter 4 | Materials and methods
Furthermore, 4 reference genes were chosen: Beta actin (ACTB), Glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate
dehydrogenase (GAPDH), Ribosomal protein L4 (RPL4) and TATA box binding protein (TBP).
The four reference genes were chosen based on the study by Nygard et al. (2007) in which the
genes were experimentally validated particularly in pig tissue.
4.5.2
Primer design
Designing the primers is an important initial step in the set-up of a qPCR experiment.
A
complete list of the included genes can be found in Appendix F. The genes were classiﬁed
according to known functions related to DM and DN (See Figure 4.4). The sequence of all genes
was acquired through NCBI (www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed). The NCBI primer designing
tool, Primer-Blast (www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/tools/primer-blast/) was then used to obtain
the forward and reverse primer sequences. The forward and reverse primer sequence can be
found in Appendix G. The speciﬁc settings used for obtaining the desired primer sequences in
Primer-Blast can be found in Appendix H.
Figure 4.4 Gene function. Gene classiﬁcation according to known functions related to DM and DN.
Abbreviations: EMT, epithelial mesenchymal transition; TGFB, transforming growth factor beta; SMAD,
mothers against decapentaplegic.
4.5.3
qPCR primer evaluation
To ensure eﬃcient and accurate quantiﬁcation of the target template, qPCR assays should always
be optimised and validated for quantitative range and speciﬁcity by melting curve analysis.
qPCR reactions of all primer pairs were initially carried out on an Mx3005P platform (Agilent
Technologies). For evaluation purposes, a stock of pooled cDNA was used as well as two random
cDNA samples (in replicates), a -RT sample and an NTC. The qPCR reaction was initiated
30
==PAGE:53==
4.5. Quantitative real-time PCR
with a temperature of 95°C for 5 min to denature the DNA followed by 40 cycles of decrease in
temperature from 95°C to 60°C in order for annealing and extension to occur simultaneously.
After the ﬁnal PCR cycle, a melting curve analysis were performed. The thermal setup can be
found in Appendix I.
Evaluation of the primers (forward and reverse) was done at a concentration of 10µM together
with SYBR Green (Qiagen) to investigate if any unwanted formation of primer-dimer occurred
and to evaluate possible presence of unspeciﬁc amplicons. The ideal primer pair yields a Cq
between approximately 20 and 33 and has a melting curve that shows a single product (single
peak). The data obtained from the assays were analysed by the MxPro software package. The
Cq values and melting curve assessment can be found in Appendix J.
4.5.4
qPCR
qPCR was performed on the BioMarkTM HD platform (Fluidigm) using a Flex Six IFC chip
and a 96.96 IFC chip. The method is comprised of several steps: (a) pre-ampliﬁcation, (b)
exonuclease treatment, (c) Flex Six IFC chip run and (d) 96.96 IFC chip run.
a) Pre-ampliﬁcation
Pre-ampliﬁcation of target DNA was done by making a master-mix of the following (per sam-
ple): 0.5µL 250nM primer pool (forward and reverse primers in DNA suspension buﬀer), 2.5µL
2X TaqMan PreAmp Master Mix (AB) and 10.5µL water. 3.8µL master-mix was aliquoted in
a 96-well PCR plate and 1.25µL 16x diluted cDNA was added to give a total volume of 5µL.
The plate was sealed, spun down and placed in a S1000TM Thermal Cycler (Bio-Rad) with the
following conditions:
Hold 95°C 10 min.
16 cycles 95°C 15 sec, 60°C 4 min.
Hold 4°C forever
b) Exonuclease treatment
After the pre-ampliﬁcation, the 96-well plate was vortexed and centrifuged. Then, the samples
were cleaned up by exonuclease treatment. A master-mix was made, containing (per sample):
1.4µL water, 0.2µL Exonuclease I Reaction buﬀer and 0.4µL Exonuclease I 20 units/µL. 2µL
master-mix was added to each pre-ampliﬁed sample and the plate was vortexed, centrifuged and
placed in the S1000TM Thermal Cycler (Bio-Rad) with the following conditions:
Digest 37°C 30 min.
Inactivate 80°C 15 min.
Hold 4°C forever
The pre-ampliﬁed, exonuclease treated cDNA was spun down and 1µL of each sample was
transferred to an Eppendorf tube to make a cDNA pre-ampliﬁed pool for the standard curve.
The 5-fold standard curve (plus some additional concentrations) was diluted in TE-buﬀer to the
relative concentrations: 1; 0.1; 0.2; 0.5; 0.04; 0.008; 0.0016.
3µL of each pre-ampliﬁed, exonuclease treated cDNA sample was mixed with 12µL TE-buﬀer
to make a 5x dilution in a 96-well plate. The plate was sealed and stored at -20°C. An assay
mix was prepared by mixing (per assay): 2 µL 2X Assay Loading reagent and 0.4 µL 1X DNA
Suspension Buﬀer (TE-buﬀer). 2.4µL assay mix was aliquoted to a 96-well PCR plate and 1.6µL
12.5µM primer mix was then added for an assay concentration of 5µM. The assay mix was then
vortexed for 20 sec and centrifuged for 30 sec. Likewise, a sample pre-mix was prepared by
31
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 4 | Materials and methods
mixing (per sample): 2µL 2X SsoFast EvaGreen w low ROX and 0.2µL Flex Six DELTAgene
Sample Reagent. 2.2µL sample pre-mix was aliquoted to a 96-well plate and 1.8µL pre-ampliﬁed,
exonuclease treated, 5x diluted samples/NTC/standard curve was added to the plate. The plate
was then vortexed for 20 sec and centrifuged for 30 sec.
c) Flex Six IFC chip
The Flex Six dynamic array IFC was primed and loaded in accordance with the protocol found
in Appendix K. The loaded chip was placed in the HX IFC controller and the priming script
was run. Afterwards, the chip was transferred to the BioMarkTM HD platform, in which the
qPCR was performed.
d) 96.96 IFC chip
The procedure of the 96.96 IFC chip was very similar to the Flex Six IFC chips procedure.
Details of priming and loading of the 96.96 IFC chip can be found in the protocol in Appendix
L.
4.5.5
Data analysis and statistics
Data obtained from the qPCR experiment were manually curated by visual inspection of all
melting curves. NTCs as well as -RT samples were accepted when they were completely clean
or when the peak was at least 5 cycles away from the sample.
The sample replicates were
inspected and were accepted when the Cq values where < 1.5 cycles apart. Samples with > 20%
of the replicates being > 1.5 cycles apart were excluded. Furthermore, the PCR eﬃciencies were
calculated by Equation 3.1 on page 19. Following the manual curation, data were pre-processed
using the GenEx software version 6.1 (GenEx, 2018). Pre-processing was performed as follows:
(1) Data were corrected for PCR eﬃciency for each primer assay individually. (Samples with an
eﬃciency of 90-110% and R2 > 0.98 were accepted) (See Appendix M). (2) Actin beta (ACBT),
TATA-box binding protein (TBP) and Ribosomal protein L4 (RPL4) were found to be the most
stable expressed reference genes in this study using both GeNorm and NormFinder, therefore, the
geometric mean of these three genes was used to normalise all samples in GenEx. (Normﬁnder
and GeNorm analysis output can be found in Appendix N.) (3) The average of cDNA technical
repeats was calculated. In cases with high standard deviation between the two cDNA replicates,
one of the replicates were excluded from the analysis based on a visual inspection of ﬂorescence
and melting curves. (4) To visualise diﬀerential gene expression, relative expression (also called
fold change) for all samples was calculated relative to the SD diet group for each primer assay.
Graphs were created with GraphPad Prism version 7.00 for Mac OS X (GraphPad Team, 2017).
(5) Data were then log2 transformed prior to analysis of variance (ANOVA), Tukey’s honestly
signiﬁcant diﬀerence (HSD) test, Pearson correlations and principal component analysis (PCA).
The statistics were calculated with the statistical software, Rstudio (RStudio Team, 2015). Gene
expression was considered signiﬁcantly diﬀerent when p<0.0005, due to Bonferroni’s multiple
testing correction, and fold change ≳2 (up-regulated) and ≲-1.5 (down-regulated). The online
tool, STRING version 10.5 was used to create protein-protein interaction networks (Szklarczyk
et al., 2017).

5
Results
In this chapter, the results and data obtained through the work of the project are presented and
described. The chapter starts with a brief recap of the diet groups included in the study, fol-
lowed by a presentation of the physical measurements and the metabolic measurements. Lastly,
the gene expression measurements are presented including a principal component analysis, a
STRING analysis and a correlation study.
The four diet groups included in this project are summarised in Table 5.1 and included a
standard diet group fed a standard diet (SD), a group fed a high fat diet (FFC), a group fed a
high fat diet, which was changed to a standard diet (FFC/SD) and ﬁnally a group induced with
diabetes and fed a high fat diet (FFCDIA). The entire diet period was 13 months.

5.1
Physical measurements
Physical measurements performed by Schumacher-Petersen (2018), establish that after 13 months
of dieting, the Göttingen minipigs fed a high fat diet became obese with increased body weight
and total body fat % compared to the minipigs fed standard diet (See Table 5.2).

The weight of the kidneys was signiﬁcantly increased in the diet groups that received the
FFC diet (FFC and FFCDIA group) compared to the other two diet groups. However, when
comparing the kidney weight to the body weight, only the FFCDIA group had a signiﬁcantly
increased kidney weight compared to the other groups (See Figure 5.1). This indicate that the
kidneys of the pigs that received the fat diet but were not induced with diabetes were heavier
due to the increased overall body weight.
The kidneys of the FFCDIA group however were
signiﬁcantly heavier unrelated to the increase in body weight. The increased kidney weight was
therefor likely a result of the induced diabetes and not the diet.

5.2
Metabolic measurements
5.2.1
Diabetes related measurements
The FFCDIA group had statistically signiﬁcant higher fasting glucose and fructosamine values
compared to the three other diet groups (See Figure 5.2). This indicates a diabetic state with
hyperglycemia, despite daily insulin treatment.
No signiﬁcant diﬀerence in glucose or fruc-
tosamine was detected between the SD group and the FFC group, indicating that diet alone did
not induce hyperglycemia (Schumacher-Petersen, 2018).

5.2.2
Renal function related measurements
Inulin clearance (Cin) is a measure of renal function and is used as an indicator of GFR since
inulin is completely ﬁltered at the glomerulus. Inulin clearance can be calculated per weight in
kg or per body surface area (BSA). BSA is a way of correcting for the size of the test subject.
The BSA of pigs is generally estimated based on the weight and the type of pig used in the study.
The formula for estimating BSA of minipigs is given below (Equation 5.1) (Swindle et al., 2012).
Miniature pigs : BSA(m2) = 0.121BW 0.575
kg
(5.1)

to the other three diet groups, indicating hyperﬁltration which is an indicator of early stage

nephropathy (See Figure 5.3). No signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the SD group and the FFC group
was observed, indicating that diet alone had no eﬀect on the inulin clearance. Inulin clearance
data were provided by Rikke Lindgaard Thomsen (personal communication, September, 2018),

Creatinine (CREA) is a chemical waste product generated from the muscle metabolism by
breakdown of creatine. CREA is transported through the bloodstream to the kidneys, where
most is ﬁltered and secreted via the urine. The measurement of CREA is used to evaluate kidney
function. Accumulation of plasma CREA is associated with a decreased GFR (Davis, 2018). A
decrease is in plasma CREA is an indication of an increased renal ﬁltration function, but it can
also be a result of poor liver function.

5.3
Gene expression measurements
5.3.1
RNA extraction and quality control
RNA was successfully extracted from all kidney samples. The purity of the RNA was measured
as the ratio between A260 and A280 and the ratio between A260 and A230. The values ranged from
2.09 to 2.14 and from 1.3 to 2.5, respectively. The quantity of extracted RNA was above 500
ng/µL for all samples except two, which were up-concentrated. The RNA quality ranged from
7.9 to 10 which conﬁrms the good integrity of all extracted RNA. The values are summarised in
Table 5.4 and the values from all samples can be found in Appendix P. The individual sample
curves from the Experion analysis can be found in Appendix Q.

Table 5.4 RNA purity, quantity and quality.
Purity and quality data presented as mean ±
standard deviation and quantity data presented as interval. Abbreviation: RQI, RNA quality indicator.
aRQI scale from 1-10. 1 being highly degraded RNA and 10 being intact RNA.
In general, a A260/A280 ratio of ∼2.0 is accepted as pure for RNA. If the ratio is lower, it
might indicate the presence of protein, phenol or other contaminants that absorb strongly at
280nm. The A260/A230 ratio for pure RNA is often higher than the A260/A280 ratio and are
considered pure in the range 2.0-2.2.
The RNA was also submitted to a visual inspection from which the RNA quality was also
deemed acceptable. Pictures of the gels can be found in Appendix R. The overall assessment of
the RNA indicate that the puriﬁed RNA was pure and of high quality with no degradation-signs.

5.3.2
qPCR
5.3.2.1
qPCR data curation
A section of the qPCR heat map is shown in Figure 5.5, with the ten genes with diﬀerential
expression between the four diet groups. The NTC and -RT samples are presented as well.
Cq
Figure 5.5 Heat map. Heat map showing the Cq value of non-normalised raw data from kidney
samples from pigs in four diet groups in addition to a non-template control (NTC) and no reverse
transcription (-RT) samples. A high gene expression corresponds to a low Cq value (yellow), while a low
gene expression corresponds to a high Cq value (purple/black).
Following visual inspection of the colour coding and melting curves, 90 reference- and kidney
related genes were expressed within the dynamic range in kidney tissue from the four diet
groups. ACOX3 and KCP were removed due to primer-dimer, CD163 and HGF were removed
due to missing ampliﬁcation product and COL3A1 and ICAM1 were removed due to high cDNA
replicate variety. Eﬃciency was calculated based on the standard curves with a minimum of
4 dilution points. The eﬃciency ranged from approximately 95% to 110%. The entire cDNA
duplicate was removed if > 20% of the genes in a speciﬁc sample had a Cq variety of > 1.5.
Eight sample replicates were removed. (See Appendix S for details.) Furthermore, the -RT and
NTC samples were inspected, and it was conﬁrmed that these did not contain ampliﬁed product.
An overview of the heat map for the entire raw data from the qPCR can be found in Appendix
T.
5.3.2.2
Distribution of results according to function
The expression proﬁle of 92 genes related to DN and 4 reference genes were analysed. A complete
list of the 96 genes is given in Appendix F. From the analysis of the expression proﬁle of 92
genes of interest, 10 genes were found signiﬁcant after correcting for multiple testing (p<0.0005,
fold change ≳2 and ≲-1.5. 32 genes were signiﬁcant at a 95% signiﬁcance level but were not
signiﬁcant after correcting for multiple testing (p<0.05). The ﬁnal 50 genes were non-signiﬁcant
(p>0.05). The distribution of the genes according to function is summarised in Figure 5.6.

when p<0.0005 as a result of Bonferroni’s correction for multiple testing (Bland and Altman,
