5.3.2.3
Principal component analysis
A principal component analysis (PCA) was performed based on data from the 10 signiﬁcantly
diﬀerent expressed genes found in the aforementioned statistical analysis. PCA is a mathematical
procedure that reduces a large number of (possibly) correlated variables into a smaller set, that
still contains most of the information of the larger set. The axes of the PCA were created based
in the span of variation within the data set and illustrates the direction of variance within the
data set. Principal component (PC) 1 has the highest span of variation and PC2 has the second
highest span of variation. The grouping and colouring was done based on treatment/diet (See
Figure 5.9).
When comparing the four diet groups, three major groups were formed, discriminating be-
tween the diabetic group (FFCDIA), the high fat diet group (FFC) and the control group (SD).
No discrimination between the control group (SD) and the diet-normalisation group (FFC/SD)
was observed.

==PAGE:65==
5.3. Gene expression measurements
5.3.2.4
STRING analysis
The interactions between the 10 signiﬁcantly expressed genes were inspected in STRING version
10.5 (Szklarczyk et al., 2017). This software allows to investigate protein-protein interaction net-
works. The active interactions included in the analysis were based on text mining, experiments,
databases and coexpression with a minimum required interaction score of low conﬁdence (See
Figure 5.10). The data are represented as a graph where each gene is a node and two nodes
are connected by an edge if the genes are interacting. The network conﬁrms and visualise the
known interactions between the signiﬁcant genes related to DN. The gene, ACE, is highly in-
terconnected in the network and has connections to 8 out of the 9 remaining signiﬁcant genes.
As expected, SMAD4 and BMP7 are connected, which is consistent with them being involved
in the TGFB/SMAD signalling pathway.
Figure 5.10 STRING network.
The interactions between the 10 signiﬁcantly expressed genes.
Green edges: computationally predicted interactions, Blue edges: information from curated databases,
pink edges: experimentally determined interactions. Node colour according to biological process, gene
ontology (GO) annotation: Red (GO:0042127) regulation of cell proliferation; blue (GO:0008285) negative
regulation of cell proliferation; green (GO:0006950) response to stress; transparent: no category.
Based on the GO annotations from STRING the genes GSTP1, CDKN1A, SMAD4 and BMP7
are involved in both regulation of proliferation and negative regulation of proliferation. 7 out of
10 genes are involved in response to stress.

Figure 5.11 Correlation matrix. Pearson correlation of genes and metabolic measurements. Blue
indicates positive correlation and red indicates negative correlation. Increased colour intensity indicate
stronger correlation. Stars indicate p-values: ***p<0.001, **p<0.01, *p<0.05. Blank spaces are not
signiﬁcant. Correlations in the range 0.10 to 0.29 or -0.29 to -0.10 are considered weak, correlations in
the range 0.30 to 0.49 or -0.49 to -0.30 are considered moderate and correlations in the range 0.50 to 1.00
or -1.00 to -0.50 are considered strong (Sindt and Jørgensen, 2017). Abbreviations: CREA, creatinine;
Cin, inulin clearance.
A strong, signiﬁcant correlation between Cin(mL/hr/kg) and Cin(mL/hr/BSA) can be seen.
The two metabolic parameters are two diﬀerent ways of calculating inulin clearance, which is a
way of measuring GFR. Based on the strong correlation, both calculation methods were equally
accurate and can substitute one another (See Figure 5.12a). Furthermore, the fructosamine and
plasma glucose measurements seem to be strongly correlated. However, a visual inspection of
the correlation (See Figure 5.12b) reveal that the correlation was in fact not very strong since
very few data point represent the area between the two dense extremes. The prerequisites of a
correlation are that the data are approximate normal distributed and have a linear relationship
(Sindt and Jørgensen, 2017).

decreased compared to the other three diet groups and both plasma glucose and fructosamine
were increased in the FFCDIA group.

==PAGE:69==
6
Discussion
In this chapter, the collected data and its biological interpretation will be discussed and com-
pared to published data and knowledge on the topic in order to explain the ﬁndings.
The
beginning of the chapter will be a short summary of the results followed by a discussion of
the deregulated genes and the pathways they are involved in. Thereafter, the potential of the
Göttingen minipig as a model of DM and DN will be elucidated and the hypothesis of this thesis
will be inspected. Finally, the limitations of this study will be described.
6.1
Summary of results
In this study, investigation of the expression of genes related to kidney function was examined in
relation to dietary eﬀects and diabetes mellitus. The physical response of the porcine model was
similar to that of humans with increased body weight and BF% as the outcome of 13 months
of a high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet. The kidneys were aﬀected by the diabetic condition
with a signiﬁcant increased weight and increased glomerular area. There were no signs of ﬁ-
brosis. Moreover, when looking at perturbance in metabolism, the diabetic state aggravated an
increase in fasting glucose and fructosamine resembling a hyperglycemic state. Investigation of
inulin clearance revealed a signiﬁcant increase in the diabetic group resembling hyperﬁltration;
indicating an early state of diabetic nephropathy. The signiﬁcant decrease in plasma creatinine
levels support the presumed hyperﬁltration.
The gene expression proﬁle in kidney tissue revealed 10 genes with signiﬁcant diﬀerence in
expression levels between the four diet groups (Standard diet group (SD), diet normalisation
group (FFC/SD), high fat diet group (FFC) and the diabetic, high fat diet group (FFCDIA)).
Fold changes between the groups are shown in Table 5.6 (re-displayed) below.
47
==PAGE:70==

*P-value<0.005.
The principal component analysis on the expression proﬁle data conﬁrmed grouping of the dif-
ferent diet groups supporting diﬀerentiation between the dietary eﬀects and the diabetic eﬀects.
No immediate grouping diﬀerences were found between the SD and FFC/SD groups, meaning
either that diet-normalisation reversed the gene expression eﬀects of the high fat diet on the
analysed genes, or that no expression changes occured in the ﬁrst seven months of the study
and that the expression changes of the analysed genes occured in the last seven months of the
study period (after diet was changed).
Based on computational predicted interactions, all 10 signiﬁcant regulated genes are connected
in a network. The connections between SMAD4 and the genes; BMP7, ACE and CDKN1A have
moreover been established experimentally, as well as the connections between NPHS2 and the
genes; NPHS1 and CD68. Almost all 10 genes are associated with stress response and regulation
of cell proliferation according to the computational predictions from STRING (https://string-
db.org).
A strong positive correlation between the metabolic parameter, plasma glucose and the ex-
pression of the gene, CDKN1A indicate that an increased expression of this gene is connected to
the hyperglycemic state. A signiﬁcant, strong correlation was also found between the expression
of the two genes; NPHS1 and VEGFB and between NPHS1 and NPHS2.
48
==PAGE:71==

Figure 6.1 The cell cycle. Cells are dormant
in the G0-phase until they enter the cell cycle in
the G1-phase due to growth factor stimuli. Under
the right conditions, the cell enter S-phase to repli-
cate it’s DNA and from there enter G2 for further
preparation in order to enter the M-phase. If the
cell is arrested in the G1-phase, the cell increases in
size but does not divide. Figure from Wolf (2000).
Cyclin dependent kinase inhibitor 1A (CDKN1A)
also known as p21, is a known cell cycle reg-
ulator, regulating the cell cycle progression at
the G1 and S-phase (Wade Harper et al., 1993).
By inhibiting cyclin A, D and E, CDKN1A is
able to arrest the cell in the G1-phase (Wade
Harper et al., 1993; Wolf, 2000).
Renal cells
have a very low turnover rate and are dormant
in the G0-phase until stimulation from a growth
factor, after which they enter the G1-phase. In
the G1-phase the cells increase in size to pre-
pare for DNA replication.
An arrest in this
phase cause the cell to increase in size with-
out being able to divide, causing hypertrophy
(See Figure 6.1). In 1998, Kuan et al. showed
that hyperglycemic induced mesangial cell hy-
pertrophy is associated with increased expres-
sion levels of CDKN1A in Sprague Dawley Rats.
From a study of mouse podocytes, increased lev-
els of CDKN1A was linked to podocyte apopto-
sis. CDKN1A is thus able to regulate podocyte
number by increasing apoptosis (Wada et al.,
2005).
The stimulating growth factor that is
mostly associated with CDKN1A activation is
TGFB. TGFB interacts with its receptor con-
sisting of a TBRI and TBRII complex to phosphorylate SMAD2 and SMAD3 in order to make
a complex with SMAD4, which in turn regulate the expression of CDKN1A (See Figure 6.2)
(Yanagita, 2012). TGFB up-regulation in injured cells are associated with localised wound-
healing responses, which, if uncontrolled, lead to progressive ﬁbrosis and kidney injury (Manson
et al., 2015). The fact that TGFB is not up-regulated in this study is in agreement with the
lack of ﬁbrosis in the renal tissue of the diabetic pics.

Figure 6.2 Up-regulation of CDKN1A. Several pathways are suggested to promote activation of

SMAD4 is a shared signalling partner of TGFB and BMP7, but BMP7 signalling cause
SMAD4 to form a complex with SMAD1, SMAD5 and SMAD8, which results in ﬁbrosis inhi-
bition and inhibition of apoptosis (Pardali et al., 2005). In a study of early renal damage in
diabetic rhesus monkeys, SMAD4 was up-regulated in the diabetic monkeys (plasma glucose be-
tween 15mmol/L) compared to control subjects after 24, 36 and 47 months (Wang et al., 2014).
Because the earliest expression measurements of the above study were made after 24 months,
it is diﬃcult to compare to the present study, where expression measurements were done after
13 months. BMP7 is part of a compensatory mechanism to reduce kidney damage by limiting
the inﬂammatory cascade and the following ﬁbrosis by limiting the release of pro-inﬂammatory
cytokines and chemokines. Simultaneous BMP7 stimulates increased levels of vasodialators to
increase the renal blood ﬂow. (Heﬁni et al., 2007). Pardali et al. (2005) suggest that BMP7 plays
a critical role in the repair mechanism during renal injury by repressing the TGFB dependent
proﬁbrotic pathway. However, prolonged renal injury of the kidney leads to a marked decrease
in BMP7 activity and increase in TGFB activity. The opposing actions of BMP7 and TGFB
provide temporary control of the wound healing process (See Figure 6.3).
Figure 6.3 TGFB and BMP7 activity during kidney repair and chronic kidney disease. Regulation
of TGFB and BMP7 provide a mechanism of kidney repair in the initial stage of DN. When the disease
progress to the chronic stage, BMP7 is no longer regulating the expression of TGFB. Figure by Manson
et al. (2015).
50
==PAGE:73==
6.3. Deregulated gene involved in recruitment and activation of macrophages
A study based on renal tissue from 30 patients with previously histologically conﬁrmed di-
agnosis of DN showed that BMP7 was strongly expressed in the early stages of DN, which
is in agreement with the present study, while low expression was recorded in the late stages
(Ivanac-Janković et al., 2015). An interesting study by Klose et al. (2011) present evidence of
an increase in CDKN1A as a result of BMP7 treatment, which demonstrate that BMP7 induces
cell cycle arrest at the G1-phase and reduce proliferation of human glioma derived cells. Similar
conclusions were drawn from a study of thyroid carcinoma cells by Franzén and Heldin (2001)
who found an increased fraction of cells arrested in the G1-phase and a corresponding decreased
fraction of cells in the S- and G2/M-phase after BMP7 stimulation.
From the knowledge of the previous mentioned studies the obvious deduction of the results
from the current study is that the increased expression levels of BMP7 in the diabetic group,
increase the expression of CDKN1A. Both BMP7 and CDKN1A are clearly up-regulated due
to diabetes induction.
Furthermore, three isoforms of the transforming growth factor beta
were investigated in this study; TGFB1, TGFB2 and TGFB3. None of them where signiﬁcant
diﬀerentially expressed, which further suggest that the activity of BMP7 modulates TGFB to
protect the kidney from injury in the initial disease stage.
Another up-regulator of CDKN1A has been discovered recently by Liu et al. (2018). They
found that glutathione S-transferase pi 1 (GSTP1) arrest the cell cycle in the G1/S-phase by
up-regulating CDKN1A and CDKN1B in liver cancer cells from human patients. In the present
study, increased mRNA levels of GSTP1 and CDKN1A were observed, which indicate that
GSTP1 is also up-regulating CDKN1A in diabetic kidney tissue.

In agreement with the ﬁndings in the current study, Feng et al. (2015) found that the
macrophage marker, CD68 was signiﬁcantly up-regulated in diabetic minipigs compared to con-
trols. The increase in CD68 is indicative of increased macrophage inﬁltration during the diabetic
state. However, in the current study signiﬁcant diﬀerence is not observed between the diabetic
group and the high fat group. This indicates that the increased expression of CD68 is also a
product of dietary eﬀects. Signalling pathways of the innate immune system is of great impor-
tance in the development of DN, but abnormal immune responses are also induced by obesity
(Feng et al., 2015). An increase in CD68 in both renal tissue isolated from obese pigs and from
obese/diabetic pigs was thus expected. In a study by Lin et al. (2012) renal toll like recep-
tor 4 (TLR4) was elevated and correlated with inﬁltrating CD68+ monocytes/macrophages in
human DN biopsies. In this study they suggest that TLR4 is a possible mediator of monocyte/-
macrophage recruitment in DN but also emphasise that the mechanisms of the immunologic
pathways are not fully understood. In the present study, TLR4 was not found signiﬁcantly
deregulated, and no correlation between CD68 and TLR4 was observed.

In the present study, angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) was found down-regulated in the
FFCDIA group compared to the FFC group. A similar expression was found in the case of ACE2
(despite the p-value not being signiﬁcant after multiple testing correction).
ACE evidently plays an important role in vasoconstriction by inactivating the vasodilator
bradykinin and converting angiotensin I to the vasoconstrictor angiotension II. By contrast,
ACE2 is involved in the promotion of vasodialation by degrading the angiotension II (See Figure

The expression of ACE has been reported down-regulated in tubular kidney tissue from di-
abetic Sprague-Dawley rats in a study by Tikellis et al. (2003) but many other contradictory
ﬁndings are reported regarding the expression of ACE and ACE2. Contradictory to the afore-
mentioned study by Tikellis et al. (2003), an increase in ACE expression and a decrease in
ACE2 expression in biopsies from patients suﬀering from DN compared to control subjects was
reported by Reich et al. (2008). Another study showed decreased levels of both ACE and ACE2
in medulla and cortex tissue from SpragueDawley rats induced with STZ compared to controls
(Velkoska et al., 2010).
The many contradictory studies make it diﬃcult to make any conclusions on whether or not
the data obtained in the present study is similar to that observed in humans. Furthermore, since
the deregulation of ACE and ACE2 in the present study was found between the FFCDIA group

6.5. Deregulated gene involved in glucose transport
and the FFC group, it is not possible to determine if the deregulation is a result of DM induction
with STZ or diet. This distinction would be possible if a diabetic group fed standard diet had
been included in this study. However, there is reported consensus that ratio between ACE and
ACE2 is important to the progression of DN because of the vasodilating and constricting eﬀects.

In the present study, solute carrier family 2 member 1 (SLC2A1) was signiﬁcant deregulated in
the FFC group compared to the FFCDIA group. No diﬀerence in expression was found between
SD and FFC.
In 2014 Guzman et al. investigated the glomerular SLC2A1 expression at diﬀerent stages of
DN. They found that SLC2A1 expression was signiﬁcantly down-regulated in glomeruli from
patients with T1DM, normoalbuminuria and high GFR compared to controls, which is in agree-
ment with the present study. Furthermore, they reported an increase in SLC2A1 expression
in patients with microalbuninuria and normal GFR (Guzman et al., 2014). The ﬁndings sug-
gest that SLC2A1 is down-regulated in early stages of DN and that the expression of SLC2A1
increase as DN develops.
SLC2A1 facilitates glucose transport across the plasma membrane and into the blood (See
Figure 6.5). When glucose levels are increased in the blood, the expression of SLC2A1 decreases
(Dominguez et al., 1994).
Figure 6.5 Glucose transport facilitated by SLC2A1 in the nephron. Glucose is reabsorbed by
SGLT1/2 and transported across the membrane by SLC2A1. Abbreviations: SGLT, sodium-glucose linked
transporter; Na+, sodium; K+, potassium; GLUT (or SLC2A), glucose transporter. Figure adapted from
Medscape Education (2014).
SLC2A1 is one of 14 in the SLC2A family. Since SLC2A1 was the only one investigated
in the present study, it is diﬃcult to obtain an overall picture of the eﬀects of DN on glucose
53
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 6 | Discussion
transport. Guzman et al. (2014) has reported up-regulation of SLC2A4 expression from patients
with T1DM, normoalbuminuria and high GFR compared to controls and a decreased expression
in patients with microalbuninuria and normal GFR, which is the opposite of the expression
they reported on SLC2A1. This indicates a complex regulation of glucose transport during the
development of DN.

==PAGE:77==
6.6. Deregulated genes involved in podocyte structure and function
up-regulation is not needed in the podocytes of the diabetic group yet. In a recent study by
Falkevall et al. (2017), VEGFB has been reported to also regulate glomerular lipid accumulation
and to induce podocyte insulin resistance in a diabetic kidney. Furthermore, the study reports
up-regulated expression levels of VEGFB in patients with chronic kidney disease and propose
a therapeutic treatment based on reducing VEGFB signalling in order to increase podocyte
insulin sensitivity and reduce intracellular lipid content (Falkevall et al., 2017).
Nephrin (NPHS1) and podocin (NPHS2) are both podocyte proteins involved in maintaining
the selective permeability of the glomerular capillary membrane.
Structural and functional
damage to the slit diaphragm between podocytes is associated with down-regulation of NPHS1
and NPHS2 (See Figure 6.7) (Chen et al., 2015).
In the present study, podocin (NPHS2) was up-regulated in the FFC and FFCDIA groups
compared to the SD and FFC/SD groups. This is in disagreement with a study by Chen et al.
(2015), who reported a decrease in NPHS2 as well as NPHS1 in rats after 4 and 8 weeks after
onset of DM compared to a control group.
Furthermore, they report a correlation analysis
where the expression of NPHS1 and NPHS2 is negatively correlated with VEGF mRNA levels.
In the present study, NPHS1 was positively correlated to VEGFB (r=0.85, p<0.001). In another
study by Ma et al. (2013), NPHS1 and NPHS2 was also reported down-regulated in 8-week old
diabetic rats compared to controls. In both studies, however, they report microalbuminuria in
the diabetic group, which is an indicator of stage III DN. This suggests that the progression
of DN was more severe in the rat studies compared to the present mini pig study. Moreover,
in the present study the diabetic group was treated with insulin, which is not the case in the
two aforementioned studies. Baelde et al. (2004) conducted a gene expression proﬁle done on
cadaveric donor kidneys from deceased diabetic nephropathy patients. They reported decreased
expression of NPHS1 and VEGF in agreement with Chen et al. and Ma et al. but in disagreement
with the present study. However, the patients suﬀered from DM for >5 years and a severely
decrease GFR was reported. The study thus represents the later stages of DN.
Figure 6.7 Slit diaphragm proteins involved in the structure and functions of the podocytes.
Decreased expression of NPHS1 and NPHS2 are associated with increased albuminuria as a result of
impaired structural integrity of the slit diaphragm. Figure adapted from Quaggin (2009) and Luhidan
et al. (2016).
In the present study, it is obvious to assume that that the podocytes have maintained their
structure and function which correspond to the lack of microalbuminuria in the minipigs. It is
55
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 6 | Discussion
very likely that the expression levels of NPHS1 and NPHS2 would decrease and expression of
VEGFB would increase if the study period had been extended and the podocytes had suﬀered
from more injury.
6.7
Is the Göttingen minipig model a good model of DN in
humans?
In essence, the minipig model described in this project eﬃciently displayed the early changes
associated with stages of DN in humans. The ﬁltration rate, based on inulin clearance and
serum creatinine, was signiﬁcantly increased, and urinary excretion of albumin was unchanged.
Based on these observations, the expression proﬁle from this study is presenting the early phase
of the disease.
This means that during the early phase of the disease the kidney reacts by
deregulating genes involved in a controlled repair mechanism, such as up-regulating BMP7
in order to prevent progression of the disease.
In the later stages of the disease BMP7 is
gradually down-regulated, suggesting that the kidney’s prevention mechanism gradually becomes
uncontrolled and abnormal during the progression of DN (Cortes and Mogensen, 2006). The
kidneys are able to stay functional for a period before gradually becoming impaired with a
decrease in the ﬁltration function (See Figure 6.8).
Figure 6.8 The phases of diabetic nephropathy. In the initial stage, ﬁltration rate is increased
and urinary protein excretion is unchanged. During progression of the disease, the ﬁltration rate decline
and the urinary excretion of protein increase. Figure adapted from Rossing (2015).
6.8
Hypotheses
The ﬁrst hypothesis to be discussed is whether a high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet has
induced changes in the kidney transcriptome.
Based on the qPCR analysis, 10 out of 92 genes showed signiﬁcant changes (p<0.0005) in
gene expression between the diet groups. 32 genes were signiﬁcant (p<0.005) but did not stay
signiﬁcant after correcting for multiple testing. To evaluate whether or not the change in expres-
sion was due to the high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet, a Tukey’s HSD test was performed
56
==PAGE:79==
6.9. Limitations
to establish the signiﬁcant changes between the individual diet groups. Signiﬁcant changes in
gene expression related to a high fat diet were found in seven out of the 10 important genes.
The high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet has induced changes in the kidney transcriptome,
therefore the hypothesis can be accepted.
The second hypothesis to be discussed is whether the experimental induction of DM with STZ
in minipigs fed a high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet induced speciﬁc changes in the kidney
transcriptome related to DM and DN in humans. Based on the metabolic measurements which
indicate hyperglycemia, the minipigs are assumed to have attained a diabetes-like state. Out
of the 10 signiﬁcant deregulated genes seven genes are deregulated between the diabetic group
and one or more of the other groups. The experimental induction of DM with STZ in minipigs
fed a high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet has therefore induced speciﬁc changes in the kidney
transcriptome.
The question of whether the changes in the diabetic minipig kidney transcriptome are related
to the changes reported in the diabetic human kidney transcriptome is diﬃcult to answer. The
diﬃculty lies in the diﬀerent stages of the disease. Most human studies are based on samples
from patients with more progressed DM and DN with decreased GFR and increased urinary
excretion of proteins, since these are the clearest symptoms used for diagnosing DN (Kato and
Natarajan, 2015). Many animal studies have provided evidence of diﬀerence in the transcrip-
tome between early stages and later stages of DN. The present study, which demonstrate the
initial stage of the disease and studies of more advanced DM and DN are deemed incomparable
and the hypothesis can only be partially accepted.
The third and ﬁnal hypothesis to be discussed is whether the glomerular ﬁltration rate of
diabetes induced minipigs fed a high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet was increased as seen
in early stages of diabetic nephropathy in humans. This hypothesis is accepted based on the
metabolic measurements that provide evidence of a signiﬁcant increase in inulin clearance as a
measure of GFR. Furthermore, plasma creatinine levels were found to be signiﬁcant lower in the
diabetic group, which is an indicator of increased GFR because the creatinine is ﬁltered from
the blood more rapidly than in the other three diet groups.
6.9
Limitations
11 out of 54 animals were terminated prematurely and no samples of kidney tissue were taken
from those animals. It was thus not investigated if the early terminated minipigs were more
challenged by severe changes compared to the surviving minipigs.
The most aﬀected group
was the diet group, FFCDIA which originally included 14 animals and unfortunately six of the
animals were terminated early. This is an unfortunate limitation to the analysis because the
sample number from this diet group was substantially reduced and the prematurely terminated
animals might have been the ones most eﬀected by the diet and STZ induction.
Kidney biopsies taken during the 13 months study period would have provided valuable in-
formation on the development and progression of the kidney disease. Especially biopsies from
the FFC/SD group before and after diet was changed would be useful to evaluate the eﬀects of
the diet change. In this study, no signiﬁcant diﬀerence was detected in the expression proﬁles
between the two groups: SD and FFC/SD. The lack of diﬀerence in the expression proﬁles might
suggest that the ﬁrst seven months of the diet period has no eﬀect on the results. However, it
could also indicate that the kidneys are able to reverse the eﬀect of the ﬁrst seven months of
diet or that the changes happen in other genes than those proﬁled in this study. It would be
57
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 6 | Discussion
interesting to investigate the matter, which is unfortunately not possible since kidney biopsies
were only done at the termination of the study. A diabetic group fed a standard diet would
make it possible to diﬀerentiate the role of the high fat, fructose, and cholesterol diet in the
diabetic group. Then it would be possible to investigate the magnitude of inﬂuence diet has on
the diabetic kidney.
Another limiting factor is the statistical correction for multiple testing with Bonferroni’s
method. This method was used to correct the probabilities in the expression proﬁle from the
qPCR experiment. The method is widely used to decrease the probability of type I errors (re-
jecting the null hypothesis when it is true). It is however impossible to decrease the probability
of type I errors without inﬂating the probability of type II errors (failing to reject the null hy-
pothesis when it is false). Type II errors are no less false than type I errors (Perneger, 1998).
However, in this project, it was decided that type II errors were more acceptable than type I
errors, which means that false positives are unlikely, but false negatives are likely present. The
choice is also based on the time limit of the study, which makes 42 signiﬁcant genes (with a
high probability of type I errors) much less manageable than 10 genes (with a high probability
of type II errors). In Appendix X, volcano plots of the qPCR expression data are presented to
provide an overview of the fold changes and p-values of the genes. The plots show, that some of
the genes that are rejected due to Bonferroni’s method have a high fold change and might have
been the subject of a type II error.
An important limiting factor to mention, is the number of genes including in this study. Out
of approximately 20,000 protein-coding genes only 92 was analysed, which of course gives a
limited picture of the expression proﬁle.
58
==PAGE:81==
7
Conclusion
In this chapter the conclusion of the entire project will described. Conclusions on the study
design and setup as well as the potential of the Göttingen minipig as a model of DM and DN
will be outlined.
It is evident that the gene expression proﬁle of the minipigs after one year of induced DM was
very diﬀerent from the expression proﬁle reported of advanced stages of DN. In the initial stage,
the kidney is activating mechanisms to repair and maintain its structure and function. While
the mechanisms are fully controlled, the kidney remain functional. However, once the repair
mechanisms become abnormal or uncontrolled, the organs gradually start to lose their structure
and function. Because the analysis of the transcriptome was based on tissue in a phase where
the functions are still controlled and likely overcompensating in order to maintain function, the
results are a lot diﬀerent from what would be expected from a transcriptome analysis from
kidneys with lost structure and function (ﬁbrosis, decreased GFR and increased urine protein
excretion). This is clearly demonstrated when comparing the deregulated genes of this study
with studies of early versus advanced DN.
In conclusion, the minipig has a very good potential especially to model the early stage of
DN. The study setup of this project provided insightful results of the eﬀects diet and diabetes
has on the kidneys. The combination of data from other projects on the same pigs provided by
Berit Østergaard Christoﬀersen and Rikke Lindgaard Thomsen and the gene expression data
obtained in this project made it possible to get a detailed picture of the complex disease stage.
Regarding the chosen animal model, the Göttingen minipig eﬀectively mimicked the early stages
of human DM and DN. The physical and metabolic measurements corresponded to the levels
observed in human DN in the initial period after onset of DM. The expression proﬁle revealed
genes, which have been associated with DM and DN in many animal and human studies (See
Table 7.1).

==PAGE:83==
8
Future objectives
The ﬁnal chapter of this thesis will be focused on perspectives of the project and suggestions for
future research.
The Göttingen minipig display convincing qualities of a good model of human DM and early
signs of DN, however, a porcine study of a longer duration and more developed DN is needed in
order to compare the porcine model to the many human studies conducted on patients suﬀering
from more advanced stages of DN. It would be interesting to develop a minipig model of more
advanced DN with complications such as ﬁbrosis and decreased GFR.
Moreover, kidney biopsies from various time points during the study period would provide a
more detailed picture of the expression proﬁle during the progression of DN. An interesting idea
is to develop a minipig model of all ﬁve stages of DN, if possible, in order to map the entire
pathway of DN. This way it would be possible to investigate diﬀerent therapeutic methods for
treating or curing the various stages of DN in human patients. Although it would be most
eﬀective to cure DN in the initial stage, it has to be considered that a lot of people are unaware
of their diabetic condition and the sequelae. It has been estimated by Beagley et al. (2014)
that 174.8 million people globally suﬀered from undiagnosed DM in 2013. For this reason, the
optimal solution would be to have therapeutics for both initial stages of DN as well as late
stages. For future studies of DN it would also be interesting to include a diabetic group fed a
standard diet to be able to determine how much inﬂuence diet has on the diabetic kidney. This
is an important distinction because DM is a multifactorial disease where, especially in T2DM,
environmental factors, such as diet, has an enormous impact. In the present study, 92 genes
were investigated, but many other genes are likely very important in the pathogenesis of the
disease. As a suggestion, the entire cell cycle pathway would be interesting to investigate as well
as all genes involved in the TGFB/SMAD signalling pathway.
The possibilities of this model are endless and there are many opportunities to improve and
expand the study setup in order to uncover the entire pathogenesis of DM and DN, which will
beneﬁt a very large, and increasing part of the population suﬀering from this disease.

==PAGE:99==
A
Inulin clearance - protocol
The inulin clearance test procedure was provided by Berit Østergaard Christoﬀersen, Novo Nordisk
A/S.
77
==PAGE:100==
 
Inulin clearance and IVGTT 
 
Fasting:  
 
The pigs are fasted for approx. 18 hours prior to testing. The pigs are fed their normal food 
ratio after the last blood sample has been obtained in all animals. 
The other pigs in the stable are fasted the same way as the pigs in test. 
 
Insulin:  
 
Baseline: The pigs with diabetes are not given their insulin dose the day before and on the 
day of the testing. 
 
Post intervention: On the test day the pigs with diabetes are given their insulin dose after 
the test is finish, and they have eaten their food. 
 
Dosing: 
 
Both Glucose and Inulin is given as an I.V. bolus though the intravenous catheter. 
 
1. Glucose (0.3 g/kg => 0.6 ml/kg) is givens first, followed by 5 mL of sterile saline. 
 
2. Then the inulin injection is given (Baseline: 5 mg/kg bodyweight => 0.2 ml/kg 
bodyweight/ Post intervention: 2.5 mg/kg body weight => 0.1 ml/kg) followed 
by 10 ml of sterile saline.  
 
The time starts when the inulin dose is finished. The time used from starting the glucose bolus, 
until the inulin bolus injection is completed, is registered in the raw data. 
 
Inulin: 
 
Inulin-FITC, 5 g from TdB Consultansy AB, Sweden. Cat. No. FI.    
 
Preparation of 25 mg/ml Inulin-FITC solution (in the morning of the study): 
 
1. 2.5 g FITC-inulin is dissolved in 100 mL of sterile 0.9% NaCl in a glass container – 
dissolution is facilitated by heating the solution on a heating plate up to 70oC. The 
concentration is now 25 mg/ml. 
 
2. Prior to use, this solution is sterilized by filtration through a 45 µm filter  
 
The solution should be kept at room temperature until dosing but not kept more than XX 
hours. 
 
Blood sampling and assay: 
 
Blood (1.2 ml) is collected in tubes containing EDTA according to the schedule below and kept 
on wet ice until centrifugation. The blood samples are centrifuged at 2000G for 10 min at 4oC 
within ½ h after sampling. 
 
Ketone bodies; are measured from full blood by FreeStyle Precision Beta-Ketone-measurer + 
sticks (Abbott, UK) before dosing. 
 
Plasma is divided into following tubes for the following markers: 
 
==PAGE:101==
 
 
 
Inulin:  
100 µl is pipetted into Micronic tubes for measurement of Inulin. This includes the Pre-dose to 
5 hour samples. 
An aliquot of 200 µl of the inulin test solution is sent for analysis together with the inulin 
plasma samples. 
Assay: Inulin for GFR (FITC) in Plasma - Not validated (Flourence assay by TSD).  
 
Insulin and C-peptide: 
100 µl is pipetted into Micronic tubes for measurement of porcine Insulin and C-peptide 
(casette), the Pre-dose to 1.5 hour samples.  
Assay: Pig insulin in Pig plasma (LOCI) and Pig c-peptide in Pig plasma (LOCI), both assay are 
analysed by JFF. 
 
Extra: 
150 µl is pipetted into Micronic tubes for possible further analysis, the Pre-dose to 5 hour 
samples 
 
Glucose:  
10 µl of plasma is pipetted into a pre-filled 1.5 ml eppendorf tube of 500 µl of EBIO solution for 
measurement of plasma glucose on a Biosen auto analyser (BIOSEN S_Line, EKF Diagnostics, 
Cardiff, UK) Glucose is measured from Pre-dose to 1.5 hour. 
 
Due to logistical challenges, the baseline glucose measurements in TrPL141002 were 
transferred to Tåstrup after the 1.5 hr and the glucose measurement is performed there.  
For the Post Intervention test in TrPL141002, the measurement is performed at Frederiksberg. 
 
50 µl is pipetted into Micronic tubes for back-up for the glucose measurement, from Pre-dose 
to 1.5 hr (Only Baseline). If a glucose sample should be re-analysed, the plasma from this set 
is used. After the glucose measurement, these samples can be thrown away.

B
Puriﬁcation of total RNA from animal cells using spin
technology - protocol
Quick-Start Protocol Part 1 and 2 (Qiagen, 2016).
81
==PAGE:104==
 
 
Sample to Insight__ 
Quick-Start Protocol  
March 2016 
RNeasy® Mini Kit, Part 1 
The RNeasy Mini Kit (cat. nos. 74104 and 74106) can be stored at room temperature  
(15–25°C) for at least 9 months if not otherwise stated on label. 
Further information 
z RNeasy Mini Handbook: www.qiagen.com/HB-0435 
z Safety Data Sheets: www.qiagen.com/safety 
z Technical assistance: support.qiagen.com 
Notes before starting 
z If purifying RNA from cell lines rich in RNases, or tissue, add either 10 µl β-mercaptoethanol 
(β-ME), or 20 µl 2 M dithiothreitol (DTT),* to 1 ml Buffer RLT. Buffer RLT with β-ME or DTT 
can be stored at room temperature for up to 1 month. 
z Add 4 volumes of ethanol (96–100%) to Buffer RPE for a working solution. 
z Remove RNAlater® stabilized tissue from the reagent using forceps. 
z For RNeasy Protect Mini Kit (cat. nos. 74124 and 74126), please start with the Quick-
Start Protocol RNAlater RNA Stabilization Reagent, RNAlater TissueProtect Tubes, and 
RNeasy Protect Kits. 
* This option not included for cells in handbook; handbook to be updated. 
1. Cells: Harvest a maximum of 1 x 107 cells, as a cell pellet or by direct lysis in the vessel. 
Add the appropriate volume of Buffer RLT (see Table 1). 
Tissues: Do not use more than 30 mg tissue. Disrupt the tissue and homogenize the lysate 
in the appropriate volume of Buffer RLT (see Table 1). Centrifuge the lysate for 3 min at 
maximum speed. Carefully remove the supernatant by pipetting, and use it in step 2. 
2. Add 1 volume of 70% ethanol to the lysate, and mix well by pipetting. Do not centrifuge. 
Proceed immediately to step 3. 
==PAGE:105==
 
 
 
3. Transfer up to 700 µl of the sample, including any precipitate, to an RNeasy Mini spin 
column placed in a 2 ml collection tube (supplied). Close the lid, and centrifuge for 15 s 
at ≥8000 x g. Discard the flow-through. 
Optional: For DNase digestion, follow steps 1–4 of “On column DNase digestion” in 
Quick-Start Protocol RNeasy Mini Kit, Part 2. 
4. Add 700 µl Buffer RW1 to the RNeasy spin column. Close the lid, and centrifuge for 15 s 
at ≥8000 x g. Discard the flow-through. 
5. Add 500 µl Buffer RPE to the RNeasy spin column. Close the lid, and centrifuge for 15 s 
at ≥8000 x g. Discard the flow-through. 
6. Add 500 µl Buffer RPE to the RNeasy spin column. Close the lid, and centrifuge for 2 min 
at ≥8000 x g. 
Optional: Place the RNeasy spin column in a new 2 ml collection tube (supplied). 
Centrifuge at full speed for 1 min to dry the membrane. 
7. Place the RNeasy spin column in a new 1.5 ml collection tube (supplied). Add 30–50 µl 
RNase-free water directly to the spin column membrane. Close the lid, and centrifuge for 
1 min at ≥8000 x g to elute the RNA. 
8. If the expected RNA yield is >30 µg, repeat step 7 using another 30–50 µl of RNase-free 
water, or using the eluate from step 7 (if high RNA concentration is required). Reuse the 
collection tube from step 7. 
Table 1. Volumes of Buffer RLT for sample disruption and homogenization

==PAGE:106==
 
 
Sample to Insight__ 
Quick-Start Protocol  
April 2016 
RNeasy® Mini Kit, Part 2 
The RNeasy Mini Kit (cat. nos. 74104 and 74106) can be stored at room temperature  
(15–25°C) for at least 9 months if not otherwise stated on label. 
Further information 
z RNeasy Mini Handbook: www.qiagen.com/HB-0435 
z Safety Data Sheets: www.qiagen.com/safety 
z Technical assistance: support.qiagen.com 
Notes before starting 
On-column DNase digestion 
z If using the RNase-Free DNase Set for the first time, prepare DNase I stock solution by 
injecting 550 µl RNase-free water into the DNase I vial using an RNase-free needle and 
syringe. Mix gently by inverting the vial. Do not vortex. 
z For long-term storage of DNase I stock solution, divide it into single-use aliquots and store 
at –20°C for up to 9 months. Thawed aliquots can be stored at 2–8°C for up to 6 weeks. 
Do not refreeze aliquots after thawing. 
1. Add 350 µl Buffer RW1 to RNeasy column, close lid, centrifuge for 15 s at ≥8000 x g 
(≥10,000 rpm). Discard flow-through. 
2. Add 10 µl DNase I stock solution (see above) to 70 µl Buffer RDD. Mix by gently 
inverting the tube. Centrifuge briefly. 
3. Add DNase I incubation mix (80 µl) directly to RNeasy column membrane, and place on 
benchtop (20–30°C) for 15 min. 
4. Add 350 µl Buffer RW1 to RNeasy column, close lid, centrifuge for 15 s at ≥8000 x g. 
Discard flow-through. Continue with step 5 of “RNA purification from cells/tissue samples” 
in Quick-Start Protocol RNeasy Mini Kit, Part 1, or step 4 of “RNA cleanup” (below). 
==PAGE:107==
 
 
 
Notes before starting 
RNA cleanup 
z Add 4 volumes of ethanol (96–100%) to Buffer RPE for a working solution. 
1. Adjust the sample to a volume of 100 µl with RNase-free water. Alternatively, follow 
steps in “DNase digestion of RNA before RNA cleanup” in Appendix E of RNeasy Mini 
Handbook. Add 350 µl Buffer RLT, and mix well. 
2. Add 250 µl ethanol (96–100%) to the diluted RNA, and mix well by pipetting. Do not 
centrifuge. Proceed immediately to step 3. 
3. Transfer the sample (700 µl) to an RNeasy Mini spin column placed in a 2 ml collection 
tube (supplied). Close the lid. Centrifuge for 15 s at ≥8000 x g. Discard the flow-through.  
Optional: If performing optional on-column DNase digestion, follow steps 1–4 of “On 
column DNase digestion” (above) after this step. 
4. Add 500 µl Buffer RPE to the RNeasy spin column. Close the lid. Centrifuge for 15 s at 
≥8000 x g to wash the membrane. Discard the flow-through. 
5. Add 500 µl Buffer RPE to the RNeasy spin column. Close the lid. Centrifuge for 2 min at 
≥8000 x g to wash the membrane. 
Optional: Place the RNeasy spin column in a new 2 ml collection tube (supplied). Close 
the lid, and centrifuge at full speed for 1 min. 
6. Place the RNeasy spin column in a new 1.5 ml collection tube (supplied). Add 30–50 μl 
RNase-free water directly to the spin column membrane. Close the lid, and centrifuge for 
1 min at ≥8000 x g to elute the RNA. 
7. If the expected RNA yield is >30 µg, repeat step 6 using another 30–50 µl of RNase-free 
water. Alternatively, use the eluate from step 6 (if high RNA concentration is required). 
Reuse the collection tube from step 6.

cDNA synthesis (mRNA) - protocol
In-house protocol provided by associate professor, Susanna Cirera (Department of Veterinary
and Animal Sciences, University of Copenhagen).
Products to use: ImProm II (Promega); RNasin Ribonuclease inhibitor (Promega); oligdT:Random
Primer 1:3 (TAG Copenhagen); dNTPs (Amersham-Pharmacia).
Initial amount of total RNA-DNase treated from 500ng. Do two replicates of each RNA stock.
1. Mix for each sample:
1 REACTION OF
500ng OF TOTAL RNA
RNA
1µL
oligo:Random Primer 1:3 (0.5µg/µL)
0.5µL
Sdw-RNase free
1µL
Vtotal
2.5µL
2. Heat 5 min at 70°C.
3. Chill immediately on an ice-bath for 5 minutes. Spin-down. Hold on ice until addition of
master mix.
4. Add to each sample the following amount of master-mix (work on ice when making it):

==PAGE:121==
H
Primer design - settings
The primers were designed using the Primer Blast tool (www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/tools/primer-
blast).
Default setting were applied with the exception of the yellow highlighted areas to get primers
with a PCR product size between 100-150, a melting temperature between 59-63 with a max
diﬀerence of 3 and a primer size between 20-22 base pairs. The primer pairs were designed
to not span an exon-exon junction but to be separated by at least one intron. Primers were
furthermore designed to allow ampliﬁcation of mRNA splice variants.
99

==PAGE:129==
K
Fluidigm FLEXsix IFC chip - protocol
Version 29/08/2016
In-house protocol provided by associate professor, Susanna Cirera (Department of Veterinary
and Animal Sciences, University of Copenhagen).
Reagents and materials:
• Low EDTA TE, DNAse and RNase free (DNA suspension buﬀer)
• RNAse free water
• 100µM primer stocks
• 16x dilutions of cDNA samples
• 2x TaqMan PreAmp Master Mix (AB) or Fluidigm PreAmp Master mix (Fluidigm)
• Exonuclease I (NEB) + Buﬀer
• SsoFAst EvaGreen SuperMix with Low ROX (Bio-Rad)
• FlexSix Deltagene sample Loading reagent (Fluidigm)- Do not vortex
• 2x Assay Loading Reagent (Fluidigm)
• 96 well plates and tips
All primers should be mixed (forward and reverse) from stock solutions of 100µM. One NTC
(cDNA synthesis without PAP for microRNAs or cDNA synthesis without RT for mRNAs) per
tissue should be included. All cDNA used, including NTC should be 16x diluted.
1. Pooling forward and reverse primer for each assay (qPCR station):
You need to make a pool with the reverse and forward primer for each assay you are going
to run. You need to mix equal volumes of the stocks for the forward and reverse primers
for each speciﬁc gene.
-i.e. 50µL of miRX-forward primer at 100µM + 50µL of miRX-reverse primer at 100µM.
This will be called miRX primer mix 50µL
-i.e. 3µL of primer mix at 50µM + 9µL TE. This will be called miRX primer mix 12.5µM
(make it in 96 well plate). To be used later on the Fluidigm run.
2. Making a Pool of primer mix at 250nM (qPCR station):
107

12µL

Vtotal

Primer pool ﬁnal concentration 250nM.
You can also make a pool with the 96 assays that you plan to run in the 96.96 chip and
used here with 12 samples only:

Samples 16x diluted and NTCs must be preampliﬁed and cleaned by Exo I treatment
before running a chip. Preampliﬁcation is recommended in order to increase the number
of copies of target DNA. Two diﬀerent protocols according to the master mix:
1 sample

Aliquot 3.75µL STA mix for each sample and add 1.25µL 16x diluted cDNA for each sam-
ple = 5µL total. If you have 96 samples work with a plate otherwise use 0.5µL epp. tubes
when working with Flex-six. Vortex and centrifuge. Put the tubes in the PCR machine.
PCR conditions
Hold 95°C 10 min
10-14 cycles* 95°C 15 sec, 60°C 4 min
Hold 4°C forever
Spin down after PCR
*Better 10 cycles for miRNA or 14 cycles for mRNA
4. CLEAN UP of the samples=Exonuclease I treatment (qPCR station)
Dilute the exonuclease I to 4U/µL
108
==PAGE:131==
1 sample
12 samples (14)
Water
1.4µL
19.6µL
Exonuclease I Reaction buﬀer
0.2µL
2.8µL
Exonuclease I 20 units/µL
0.4µL
5.6µL
Vtotal
2µL
28µL
Add 2µL to each preAmpliﬁed sample (from step 3), vortex and centrifuge.
Vt = 7µL
Thermal cycler conditions
Digest 37°30 min
Inactivate 80°15 min
Hold 4°forever
Spin down the tubes.
Take 1µL of each pre-ampliﬁed and Exo I treated sample in order to have a cDNA pre-
ampliﬁed pool to make the standard curve.
Make tubes with 12µL TE buﬀer in each well. Take 3µL of each sample and transfer to
the new tubes to make a 5 fold dilution of the pre-ampliﬁed cDNA samples.
NOTE: If the Cq values are consistently below 6 this may need to be increased to 10-fold
or 20-fold.
Use immediately or store at -20°until use. Store the tubes with the rest of the undiluted
pre-ampliﬁed cDNA at -20°and save for backup of the experiment.
How to make the standard curve
a) For a 5 fold standard curve (for microRNAs) dilute 5µL cDNA preampliﬁed pool in
20µL TE-buﬀer and continue the dilution series until you have 7 points for the standard
curve. The relative concentrations for a 5 fold standard curve are (1; 0.2; 0.04; 0.008;
0.0016; 0.00032; 0.000064)
b) For a 10 fold standard curve (for mRNAs): dilute 1µL cDNA preampliﬁed pool in 9µL
TE-buﬀer and continue the dilution series until you have 7 points for the standard curve.
The relative concentrations for a 10 fold standard curve are (1, 0.1, 0.01, 0.001, 0.0001)
Store at -20°until use.
NOTE: Before running a 96.96 chip is a VERY good idea to run the standard curve, NTC
and some control samples on a 12.12 FLEXsix assay!
5. Preparing the Assay Mix (Fluidigm station)
Use the 12.5 µM primer mix you made in the beginning
1 inlet
12 reactions (14)
2X Assay Loading reagent
2µL
28µL
1X DNA Suspension Buﬀer (TE-buﬀer)
0.4µL
5.6µL
Vtotal
2.4µL
33.6µL
Aliquot 2.4 in each tube prepared (12 tubes). Add 1.6 µl 12.5 µM primer mix to each well.
Vt=4 µl. Each assay now is 5µM.
109
==PAGE:132==
Appendix K | Fluidigm FLEXsix IFC chip - protocol
Vortex the assay mix tubes for min 20 s and centrifuge for 30 s.
6. Preparing Sample Pre-Mix (Fluidigm station)
1 reaction
12 reactions (15)
2X SsoFast EvaGreen w low ROX
2µL
30µL
FLEXsix DELTAgene Sample Reagent (no vortex)
0.2µL
3µL
Vtotal
2.2µL
33µL
Aliquot in tubes 2.2 µl sample Pre-Mix in each tube and add 1.8 µl STA and PreAmp
cleaned and 5-fold diluted sample (or the NTC or a point of the standard curve) to each
tube. Vt= 4 µl . Vortex 20 sec and centrifuge 30 sec. Store short time at 4řC or use
immediately.
BE CAREFULL WITH BUBLES, REMOVE ALL OF THEM when the volume is trans-
ferred to the chip.
7. Priming and loading the FLEX-six dynamic array IFC
(a) If ﬁrst use of new chip, inject control line ﬂuid in each accumulator in the chip being
very careful not to pour out oil.
(b) Remove and discard blue protective ﬁlm from bottom of chip.
(c) Place the chip in the IFC controller HX and run priming script (153x).
It takes
around 50 minutes. This is only done the ﬁrst time you use the chip.
(d) Press Eject to remove the chip from the IFC Controller.
(e) Pipette 3µl of each assay and 3µl of each sample into their inlets of the chip using
a single channel Gilson pipet with Art tips. Avoid any bubbles by only pipetting to
ﬁrst step of the pipette, if you have some remove with a syringe needle
(f) Place chip in IFC controller and run load mix (153x) ( 50 min).
(g) Press Eject to remove the chip from the IFC Controller.
8. Running the chip
(a) Turn on the Biomark system (back of machine) AND the computer (side of machine).
(b) Start the Data Collection Software.
(c) Click. Start a new run.
(d) Remove debris from top of the chip with clear tape.
(e) Place chip in the Biomark HD system
(f) Change project settings if necessary and click next.
(g) Click load.
(h) If ﬁrst time with new ﬂex-six make a track ﬁle.
(i) Select the arrays you want to run (usually only one).
(j) Select application (Gene Expression), reference (ROX), probes (Evagreen) and click
next.
(k) Click browse and select thermal cycling protocol, GE FLEXSIX PCR-MELTv1. Auto
exposure should be selected.
(l) Click start run ( 2 hours).
(m) If there are still arrays left on the chip after the run, put the chip in the IFC controller
and run post-run script ( 15 min).
110
==PAGE:133==
L
Fluidigm 96.96 chips - protocol
Version 24/08/2016
In-house protocol provided by associate professor, Susanna Cirera (Department of Veterinary
and Animal Sciences, University of Copenhagen).
Reagents and materials:
• Low EDTA TE, DNAse and RNase free (DNA suspension buﬀer)
• RNAse free water
• 100µL primer stocks
• 16x dilutions of cDNA samples
• 2x TaqMan PreAmp Master Mix (AB) or Fluidigm PreAmp Master mix (Fluidigm)
• Exonuclease I (NEB) + Buﬀer
• SsoFAst EvaGreen SuperMix with Low ROX (Bio-Rad)
• 20X DNA binding Dye sample Loading reagent (Fluidigm)
• 2x Assay Loading Reagent (Fluidigm)
• 96 well plates and tips
All primers should be mixed (forward and reverse) from stock solutions of 100µM.
One -RT/NTC (cDNA synthesis without PAP for microRNAs or cDNA synthesis without RT
for mRNAs) per tissue and up to 44 test samples in 2 cDNA replicates can be loaded on the
96.96 chip.
All cDNA used, including NTC should be 8x or16x diluted depending on the start amount of
RNA used in the cDNA synthesis. mRNA skould be diluted 8x if cDNA was made form 200 ng
RNA. miRNA should be diluted 16x if made from 100 ng RNA.
Time management: Running a 96.96 chip can easily be done in 2-3 working days if you read the
protocol and prepare each step carefully. In the 3 days a ﬂex-six run is included.
1. Pooling forward and reverse primer for each assay (qPCR station):
You need to make a pool with the reverse and forward primer for each assay you are going
to run. You need to mix equal volumes of the stocks for the forward and reverse primers
for each speciﬁc gene.
111

-i.e. 3µL of primer mix at 50µM + 9µL TE. This will be called miRX primer mix 12.5µM
(make it in 96 well plate). To be used later on the Fluidigm run.

==PAGE:142==
==PAGE:143==
N
Reference gene analysis
Reference genes are used for normalisation in order to compensate for variation in sample
amount, reverse transcription eﬃciency, and RNA quality. A good reference gene has a sta-
ble expression among all samples and is constant in spite of application of varying treatments.
The stability is reﬂected by the standard deviation (SD) of biological replicates. The optimal
genes are the ones with the lowest SD (Genex, 2012). The Normﬁnder analysis output is shown
below.
Normﬁnder. TBP (TBP1) is the optimal gene according to Normﬁnder. RPL4 and ACTB was also
considered optimal due to their relative low standard diviation.
121
==PAGE:144==
Appendix N | Reference gene analysis
gNorm is another method used for selction of reference genes. The diﬀerence between Normﬁnder
and gNorm is that gNorm does not consider groups and all samples are treated as being from a
single treatment/population. The variability between samples are reﬂected by an M-value. The
M-value is related to the SD, but is based on diﬀerent sample sizes and is therefore not strictly
comparable (GenEx, 2018). The GeNorm analysis output is shown below.
GeNorm. TBP (TBP1) and ACTB are the optimal reference genes according to GeNorm.
122

Kidney ﬁbrosis
0.9217
0.2645
0.0832
Methods:
Histopathological assessment of pig glomeruli Three µm thin sections from paraﬃn em-
bedded pig kidneys were stained with Periodic Acid Schiﬀ(PAS) staining and scanned on 40 x
on a Nanozoomer (C12000-02, Hamamatsu). Twenty glomeruli were selected, 10 from the outer
cortex and 10 from the inner cortex. The glomeruli were assessed if the vascular pole and/or
macula densa were presented. The area of the glomerular tuft was measure using the NDP
viewer annotation tool. The data is presented as the average area of 20 glomeruli. Data and
method provided by Rikke Kaae Kirk (personal communication, November, 2018).
Digital Image Analysis for Quantiﬁcation of ﬁbrosis The slides were scanned by a
Nanozoomer 2.0 (x20). The image analysis was performed using Visiopharm Integrator Sys-
tem software (VIS; Visiopharm, Hoersholm, Denmark). An automated tissue detection protocol
was performed as previously published (Soendergaard et al., 2016). Quantiﬁcation of ﬁbrosis
stained by picro-sirius red was determined in a region of interest (ROI) restricted to the kidney
cortex. Within the ROI, a threshold analysis was performed using a FastRed_DAB FastRed
ﬁlter (0 - 130). Fibrosis was measured as percent stained area in the kidney cortex. Data and
method provided by Peter Helding Kvist (personal communication, November, 2018).

Q
Experion results
When evaluating the Experion run, the Experion Software was used. The lanes of the virtual
gel is inspected to make sure all samples, ladders and lower markers are visible and correct. A
correct, separated RNA ladder display the following: A peak at ~20 sec is the lower marker
used for normalisation, eight peaks corresponding to the RNA ladder peaks and ﬁnally a ﬂat
baseline.
Experion RNA StdSens separation of the RNA ladder. Lower marker, RNA ladder peaks are
visible and the baseline is ﬂat. (This is the ladder one from one of four chip runs. The three remaining
ladders were inspected but are not shown.)

X
Volcano plots
Volcano plots produced in R presenting the gene expression as signiﬁcance vs fold change.
The lower horizontal blue line represents p=0.05 and the upper horizontal blue line represents
p=0.005. The vertical lines represent fold change -1.5 and 2. Circle cross symbols indicate the
genes that were signiﬁcant after multiple testing correction and were within the fold change
thresholds.
